The involutive nature of the Littlewood–Richardson

commutativity bijection

O. Azenhas,∗

CMUC, Department of Mathematics, University of Coimbra

3000-501 Coimbra, Portugal

R.C. King†

Mathematical Sciences, University of Southampton,

Southampton, SO17 1BJ, England

and

I. Terada‡

Graduate School of Mathematical Sciences, University of Tokyo,

Komaba 3-8-1, Meguro-ku, Tokyo 153-8914, Japan

March 17, 2016

Abstract

Littlewood-Richardson (LR) coeﬃcients cλ

µν may be evaluated by means of sev-
eral combinatorial models, including the original LR tableaux of skew shape λ/µ
and weight ν and the LR hives with boundary edge labels λ, µ and ν. Unfortu-
nately, neither of these reveal in any obvious way the well-known symmetry property
cλ
µν = cλ
νµ. Here we introduce two maps, ρ(n) on LR tableaux and σ(n) on LR hives,
that each interchange contributions to cλ
νµ for any partitions λ, µ, ν of
lengths no greater than n, and then prove not only that each of them is a bijec-
tion, thereby making manifest the required symmetry property, but also that both
maps are involutions. The map ρ(n) involves the iterative action of deletion op-
erators on a given LR tableau of skew shape λ/µ and weight ν, that produce a
sequence of successively smaller tableaux whose consecutive inner shapes deﬁne a
certain Gelfand-Tsetlin pattern and determine a partner LR tableau of skew shape

µν and cλ

∗e-mail: oazenhas@mat.uc.pt
†e-mail: r.c.king@soton.ac.uk
‡e-mail: terada@ms.u-tokyo.ac.jp

1

λ/ν and weight µ. Similarly, the map σ(n) involves repeated path removals from
a given LR hive with boundary edge labels (λ, µ, ν) that give rise to a sequence of
hives whose left-hand boundary edge labels deﬁne the same Gelfand-Tsetlin pattern
as before, which is suﬃcient to determine a partner LR hive with boundary edge
labels (λ, ν, µ). The deletions in tableaux are organised so as to preserve the semis-
tandard and lattice permutation properties of LR tableaux, while the path removals
in hives are designed to preserve both the triangle condition on edge labels and the
hive rhombus gradient positivity conditions. At all stages illustrative examples are
provided.

1

Introduction and statement of results

Let n be a ﬁxed positive integer and let x = (x1, x2, . . . , xn) be a sequence of indetermi-
nates. Then, for each partition λ of length ℓ(λ) ≤ n and weight |λ|, there exists a Schur
function sλ(x) which is a homogeneous symmetric polynomial in the xk of total degree |λ|.
These Schur functions sλ(x) for all such λ form a linear basis of the ring Λn of symmetric
polynomials in the components of x. It follows that

sµ(x) sν(x) =Xλ

cλ
µν sλ(x) ,

(1.1)

where the coeﬃcients cλ
µν are known as Littlewood–Richardson (LR) coeﬃcients. These
coeﬃcients are independent of n. They are non-negative integers that may be evaluated by
means of the Littlewood–Richardson rule [LR34] as the number of Littlewood–Richardson
tableaux of skew shape λ/µ and of weight ν, where λ and µ specify the outer and the inner
shape, respectively, of the tableau, and the parts of ν specify the numbers of its entries k
for k = 1, 2, . . . , n. Equivalently, cλ
µν is the number of Littlewood–Richardson n-hives with
boundary edge labels speciﬁed by the ordered triple (λ, µ, ν) [KT99, Buc00], where each of
the three partitions has n parts through the inclusion if necessary of trailing zeros. These
are the two combinatorial models to be considered in this paper; see Sections 2.1 and
2.2 for details. Although logically distinct, they may be thought of as being equivalent
thanks to the existence of a bijection between LR tableaux and LR hives described by
Fulton in the Appendix to [Buc00]. The bijective map f (n) from the set LR(n) of all LR
tableaux with no more than n rows to the set H(n) of all hives of side length n is explained
in Section 2.3. (See also [PV05].)

Within these two models, the set of LR tableaux of shape λ/µ and weight ν is denoted
by LR(λ/µ, ν), and the corresponding set of n-hives by H(n)(λ, µ, ν) for any ﬁxed n ≥ ℓ(λ).
We then have

cλ
µν = #{T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν)} = #{H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν)} .

(1.2)

Unfortunately, although the deﬁnition (1.1) makes it immediately clear that cλ
νµ,
the same cannot be said of either of the combinatorial formulae in (1.2). The aim here is
to provide bijective maps ρ(n) : LR(n) → LR(n) and σ(n) : H(n) → H(n), such that ρ(n) :
T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) 7→ S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ) and σ(n) : H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) 7→ K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ),

µν = cλ

2

thereby proving the symmetry of the LR-coeﬃcients. It is further shown that these maps
are involutive.

Each LR n-hive is an equilateral, triangular graph of side length n, which may be
speciﬁed by means of vertex labels, edge labels or rhombus gradients, as described in
Section 2. It should be noted that in the edge labelling scheme an LR hive consists of
three interlocking, symmetrically oriented Gelfand–Tsetlin (GT) patterns [GT50], with
their types speciﬁed by the inner shape, weight and outer shape of the corresponding LR
tableau, as explained in Section 2.2. We would remark, in particular, that as a result
of the bijection between LR hives and LR tableaux, an LR tableau can be completely
speciﬁed by means of any one of the three GT patterns associated with an LR hive and
one additional sequence of boundary edge labels [BZ89, GZ86, KB96, HK06b, PV10].

The nature of our two maps ρ(n) on T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) and σ(n) on H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) is
that they proceed by providing sequences consisting of successively smaller LR tableaux
and of successively smaller LR hives in which the sequences of inner shapes and left-
hand boundary edges, respectively, determine the corresponding images S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ)
and K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ). They do so by specifying completely a GT pattern of type µ
associated with K. Both maps have corresponding inverses ρ(n)−1 and σ(n)−1 acting in
a reverse manner. Their action on T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) and H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν), build up
sequences consisting of successively larger LR tableaux and of successively larger LR
hives, repectively, with their inner shapes and left-hand boundary edges determined by
the GT pattern of type ν associated with H, culminating, as will be shown, in the same
images as before, namely S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ) and K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ). Thus the maps ρ(n) and
σ(n) are not only bijective, but also involutive.

1.1 Littlewood–Richardson commutativity involutions and the

gln-crystal commuter

Schur functions in n variables may be interpreted as irreducible characters of the gen-
eral linear group, GLn(C), and the Littlewood–Richardson coeﬃcient cλ
µν then has a
representation-theoretic signiﬁcance as the number of times the irreducible representation
Vλ appears in the decomposition of the tensor product Vµ ⊗ Vν of irreducible represen-
tations of GLn(C). Additionally, the Schur functions have an intersection-theoretic in-
terpretation as representatives of Schubert classes and, in the context of multiplication
in the cohomology of the Grassmannian, the Littlewood–Richardson coeﬃcient cλ
µν is the
multiplicity of the Schubert class σλ in the product σµσν of Schubert classes σµ and σν.
In both these contexts the products are manifestly commutative so that cλ

µν = cλ

νµ.

µν and cλ

So far as the equality of the numbers cλ

νµ is concerned just the existence of
any bijective map ρ between the sets they serve to enumerate may conclude the story. On
the other hand, there are situations where each element of the two sets represents some
distinguishable object, and then the action of the map ρ taking an element of one set to an
element of the other may carry a quite explicit piece of information that deserves further
investigation. The parametrisation of the connected components of the tensor product of
crystal bases Bµ ⊗ Bν is one such situation, particularly so in view of the fact that the

3

ﬂip map a ⊗ b 7→ b ⊗ a does not give an isomorphism of crystals Bµ ⊗ Bν → Bν ⊗ Bµ.

The gln-crystal version of the Littlewood–Richardson rule [Tho74, Tho78, Nak93,
Kwo09] amounts to the decomposition of the tensor product of crystals Bµ ⊗ Bν into
a disjoint union of crystals Bλ with multiplicities cλ
µν counted by the LR tableaux in
LR(λ/µ, ν). In [HK06b], Henriques and Kamnitzer realise their crystal commutor, which
had been deﬁned in [HK06a] in a more general setting, using the language of tableaux and
hives in the case of gln, where the key roles are played by the highest and the lowest weight
elements of the crystal Bλ, and the Sch¨utzenberger involution ξ which “reverses” the crys-
tal. These highest and lowest weight elements are characterised in part by two of the three
interlocking GT patterns associated with a hive, as will be made clear in Section 12.1.
The gln-crystal commutor Bµ ⊗Bν → Bν ⊗Bµ, linked in [HK06b] with the Sch¨utzenberger
involution, induces a bijection from the set of connected components isomorphic to Bλ
in Bµ ⊗ Bν to the analogous set in Bν ⊗ Bµ. Through the connection between such con-
nected components and hives, they obtain a bijective map H(n)(λ, µ, ν) → H(n)(λ, ν, µ),
inheriting its involutive nature from the crystal commutor. Thus they prove not only that
crystal tensor product multiplicities are symmetric, ensuring that cλ
νµ, but also that
this map is involutive. This map is also shown to coincide with another map constructed
using a special case of octahedron recurrence. In [DK05], instead of using Young tableaux,
Danilov and Koshevoy introduce other combinatorial objects, called arrays, and endow
them with a crystal structure to deﬁne a similar commutor.

µν = cλ

The Littlewood–Richardson commutativity bijections in [HK06b, PV10, DK05, DK08],
with exception of the bijection ρ3 described in [PV10] and originally deﬁned by the ﬁrst au-
thor in [Aze99, Aze00], have their involution property based on that of the above crystal
commutor [HK06a, DK05], which in turn is based on properties of the Sch¨utzenberger
involution.
In particular, the map ρ1 deﬁned by means of tableau switching opera-
tions [BSS96], is written as a composition of three Sch¨utzenberger involutions ([PV10],
Section 3.1), in order to conclude its involution property. In fact, Danilov and Koshevoy
show in [DK08] that their commutor, the Henriques–Kamnitzer hive and crystal com-
mutors [HK06b], and the maps ρ2 and its inverse ρ′
2 in [PV10], as well as the tableau
switching map ρ1 [BSS96], all coincide, and are therefore all involutions. The map ρ3,
was not considered by Danilov and Koshevoy. Our task here is to obtain an independent
proof of the involutive nature of ρ3, realised here through the bijective action of ρ(n) on
LR tableau and σ(n) on LR hives.

1.2 Our Littlewood–Richardson commutativity involution

In Sections 3–6, we discuss the map originally deﬁned by the ﬁrst author, which we denote
by ρ(n), taking any LR tableau T of shape λ/µ and weight ν to another such tableau S of
shape λ/ν and weight µ, where λ, µ and ν are partitions of length ≤ n, and prove that
the map ρ(n) has the involutive nature.

The map ρ(n) is introduced in Sections 3 and 4. Here we begin by outlining the
content of Section 4, only referring back to the content of Section 3 as and when necessary.
The map is deﬁned by way of transforming the tableau T = T (n) into gradually smaller

4

tableaux T (n−1), T (n−2), . . . , T (1) and ﬁnally into the empty tableau T (0), in such a way
that, upon each transition from T (r) to T (r−1), the outer shape simply loses its rth row,
the inner shape shrinks by losing a horizontal strip, the entries r are all removed but the
entries 1, 2, . . . , r − 1 are shifted in the tableau in a certain way without being lost; and
while doing so the tableau ρ(n)(T ) = S is constructed, gradually from bottom to top, in
such a way that the rth row of S is determined by the information on the change of the
inner shape from T (r) to T (r−1) along with the number of entries r removed from T (r)
(See Deﬁnition 4.1). The transformation from T (r) to T (r−1) is achieved by applying a
sequence of deletion operators (Deﬁnitions 3.1 and 3.6). The action of each such operator
is a gentle modiﬁcation of the inverse of Sagan–Stanley’s internal insertion. To be more
precise, the action starts from the rightmost corner of the bottommost row of the tableau,
and that corner is deleted in terms of the tableau’s shape. As for the entry of that corner,
it is simply removed if that entry matches the row number r, and in such a case that is the
only change made in the tableau by the operator. Otherwise a certain path is generated
starting from that corner, and the entries get shifted upwards along the path. The path
extends until reaching one of the corners of the inner shape, and an entry bumped out
of the path is placed in that corner. Moreover, this operation preserves the LR property.
Such an operation is repeated as many times as there are cells in the rth row of T (r) to
produce T (r−1). It easily follows from the deﬁnitions that the constructed tableau S has
shape λ/ν and weight µ, but in showing its LR property (Theorem 4.2), a key role is
played by the comparison results (Lemmas 3.8, 3.10 and 3.11) of the paths taken by the
deletion procedures when applied successively. An illustration of the complete procedure
of constructing S from T can be found in Example 4.4.

The map ρ(n)

is injective, since one can recover T (r) from T (r−1) along with the
inner and outer shapes of T (r), by applying internal insertions and augmenting by a
suitable number of entries r in row r. This gives the inequality of the cardinalities
|LR(λ/µ, ν)| ≤ |LR(λ/ν, µ)|. By applying the same argument with µ and ν switched, we
see that the inequality is actually an equality, and the injection is forced to be bijective
(see Theorem 4.8).

Then we prove that the map ρ(n) is an involution (Theorem 5.1). This proof is some-
what long and occupies both Sections 5 and 6. In Section 5 the proof is reduced to the
analysis, in the case where the tableau T has a single cell containing r in its bottom rth
row, of how the deletion from the bottom cell containing r “commutes” in a certain way
with the deletions starting from the cells in the (r − 1)th row, and how this commutation
relation is reﬂected in the constructed tableau S. This is spelt out in Proposition 5.8 and
the whole of Section 6 is devoted to its proof.

Let us add here that internal insertions, together with deletions, were originally used
in [Aze00] by the ﬁrst author in showing the bijectivity of ρ(n) and also in the statement
to which the proof of the involutive nature was reduced. This statement is equivalent to
Lemma 5.6, and Proposition 5.8 mentioned above is a reﬁnement of Lemma 5.6.

We turn now to our hive model and the problem of identifying a bijective map σ(n) :
H(n) → H(n) such that for all H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) we have σ(n)
: H 7→ K with K ∈
H(n)(λ, ν, µ). By virtue of the bijection f (n) from LR n-tableaux to LR n-hives introduced

5

in Section 2.3, deletions on tableaux correspond to path removals from a hive. These are
deﬁned in Section 7 in terms of the action of three types of path removal operators, χr,
φr and ωr. Each of these reduces or increases by 1 the values of certain edge labels in a
given hive. These edges form a connected path, starting from the base of the hive and
proceeding to its left or right hand boundary. The route taken by each path is determined
by the occurrences of upright rhombi of gradient 0. It is ﬁrst shown that this action on
an LR hive in H(r) always yields another LR hive in H(r).

The next step, described in Section 8, is to show that the systematic application of our
path removal operators to H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) reduces it to an n-hive involving an empty
rightmost diagonal which may be removed to give an (n−1)-hive. Iterating this procedure
reduces H to an an empty hive, and recording the succession of left-hand boundary edge
labels of the intervening hives enables us to build up a partner hive K. This procedure
which involves a succession of pairs, consisting at each stage of a hive and what we call a
truncated hive, is illustrated in Example 8.3. The main result in this section is the proof
of Theorem 8.4 in which it is stated, as required, that K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ). This allows us
to deﬁne our map σ(n) through the identiﬁcation of K with σ(n)H.

In the following Section 9 we introduce a hive path addition procedure that is used to
deﬁne a map σ(n) : H(n) → H(n) such that for any K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ) we have σ(n) : K 7→
H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν). By virtue of the bijection f (n) from LR n-tableaux to LR n-hives, it may
be noted that the insertion path operation on LR tableaux introduced in [Aze99, Aze00]
corresponds to our hive path addition procedure. The hive K determines the precise
sequence of path additions, and we successfully show in the proof of Theorem 9.3 that
the result is as claimed. This involves the use of three Lemmas concerning detailed
considerations of the relationship between successive path additions.

It is then not diﬃcult to show in Theorem 10.2 that the maps σ(n) amd σ(n) are
mutually inverse bijections on H(n). However, it is considerably more demanding to prove
that these maps are involutions. This is done in Section 10 where the approach makes
use of two hives H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) and ˆH ∈ H(n)(λ − ǫn, µ, ν − ǫk) where ˆH = ψn H
is obtained from H through the removal of single path reducing the uppermost non-
zero upright rhombus gradient, say Ukn, in the rightmost diagonal of H by 1. With this
notation a crucial step is the proof of Lemma 10.5 which states that σ(n)ψn H = φn σ(n)H.
This is conveniently illustrated in the commutative diagram of Lemma 10.5. Once again
the proof involves an iterative path removal procedure in which H and ˆH are ﬁrst reduced
in size from n-hives to (n−1)-hives. They diﬀer by edge label diﬀerences of ±1 along what
we call a line of diﬀerence, and the terminating level of this line on the left-hand boundary
deﬁnes a diﬀerence in the upright rhombus gradients of their partner truncated hives that
consist at this stage of a single diagonal. Continuing this process results in succession of
consecutive lines of diﬀerence in the hives generated from H and ˆH whose terminating
levels lead to diﬀerences of upright rhombus gradients in the consecutive diagonals, taken
from right to left, of their ﬁnal partner hives K and ˆK. This procedure is not, however,
without its problems. The removal paths in H and ˆH may intersect, bifurcate and rejoin,
and the heart of the proof is taken up with proving that these possibilities, associated
with what we call critical rhombi, are no impediment to proving Lemma 10.5. Armed

6

with this Lemma it is then straightforward to complete the proof of Theorem 10.8 stating
that σ(n) is an involution.

This completes the main part of our exercise, but in Section 11 it is pointed out that
all our hive path removal and addition procedures are driven and controlled by upright
rhombus gradients. This allows us to conclude that if K = σ(n)H and any other hive
H ′ shares the same set of upright rhombus gradients as H, then its partner K ′ = σ(n)H ′
shares the same set of upright rhombus gradients as K, regardless of the boundary edge
labels.
If follows that one can deﬁne what we call a U-system of triangular arrays of
non-negative upright rhombus gradients, on which σ(n) acts in a uniform way regardless
of the speciﬁcation of the boundary labels necessary to turn such an array into a hive. It
is shown in particular that for every set of non-negative upright rhombus gradients not
only does there always exists a corresponding dressing by means of boundary labels that
is an n-hive, but also that there is an inﬁnite set of such dressings.

Section 12 consists of the discussion of the question of coincidence of all Littlewood–
Richardson commutativity bijections, ρ1, ρ2, ρ′
2 and ρ3, as raised by Pak and Vallejo
in [PV10]. These include the Benkart et al. tableaux switching map [BSS96], referred to
as ρ1, and the Henriques-Kamnitzer crystal [HK06a] and hive [HK06b] commutor maps,
and those based on arrays due to Danilov and Koshevoy [DK05, DK08] that constitute
ρ2 and ρ′
2. The maps ρ(n) and σ(n) discussed here both correspond to the map introduced
by [Aze99, Aze00] in an attempt to provide a shortcut alternative to the tableau switching
map ρ1 of [BSS96] and referred to as the fundamental symmetry map ρ3 in [PV10]. This
section closes with an illustration in support of the coincidence of ρ1 and ρ3, an approach
to whose proof was proposed in [Aze08].

2 Notation and deﬁnitions

2.1 Partitions, Young diagrams and tableaux

A partition is a ﬁnite weakly decreasing sequence λ = (λ1, λ2, . . . , λl) of strictly positive
integers, each term of which is called a part. We use lower case Greek letters such as λ, µ,
ν, . . . to represent partitions, and for a partition λ we use λi to denote its ith part. The
number of parts of a partition λ is called its length and is denoted by ℓ(λ). The sum of
its parts is called its weight and is denoted by |λ|. Sometimes the sequence λ is identiﬁed
with a longer sequence obtained by adjoining an arbitrary number of trailing zeros. In
this situation the length ℓ(λ) still refers to the number of its strictly positive parts and we
set λi = 0 for all i > ℓ(λ). We regard an empty sequence, or a sequence whose terms are
all zero, as a special case of a partition, namely the zero partition of length 0 and weight
0.

The Young diagram D(λ) of a partition λ is deﬁned to be the subset { (i, j) ∈ N × N |
1 ≤ i ≤ ℓ(λ), 1 ≤ j ≤ λi } of N × N. We represent it as an aggregate of |λ| boxes or
cells arranged in ℓ(λ) rows left-adjusted to a vertical line with its row i (counted from
the top) consisting of λi cells for each i. We refer to the cell at the intersection of row i
and column j (counted from the left) as the cell (i, j). A corner of D(λ) is a cell (i, j)

7

of D(λ) such that its removal from D(λ) leaves the Young diagram of some partition of
weight |λ| − 1.

If λ and µ are any two partitions such that µi ≤ λi for all i then we write µ ⊆ λ. In
such a case ℓ(µ) ≤ ℓ(λ), |µ| ≤ |λ| and D(µ) ⊆ D(λ) and the pair (λ, µ) speciﬁes a skew
Young diagram D(λ/µ) of shape λ/µ. This is deﬁned to be D(λ) \ D(µ) = { (i, j) | 1 ≤
i ≤ ℓ(λ), µi < j ≤ λi }, so that λ and µ specify the outer and inner shape respectively,
and the number of cells in D(λ/µ) is |λ|−|µ|.

Example 2.1. In the case λ = (6, 5, 5, 1) and µ = (3, 3, 2) we have

D(λ) =

and

D(λ/µ) =

(2.1)

A tableau T shape λ (resp. λ/µ) is any map T:D(λ) → N (resp. D(λ/µ) → N). Such a
tableau T is represented by a Young diagram with each cell (i, j) containing the number
s as an entry if T (i, j) = s. In this context, we sometimes call an entry s ∈ N a letter.
If T is a tableau of shape λ/µ, and (i, j) ∈ D(µ), we may extend the deﬁnition of T by
saying that the cell (i, j) is empty in T (even though strictly speaking (i, j) is not a cell
of T ), or that it contains 0, and accordingly we may write T (i, j) = 0 in this case. The
weight of a tableau T is the sequence α = (α1, α2, . . . , αs, . . .) where αs is the number of
entries s occurring in T (i.e. αs = #T −1(s)), and, just as in the case of partitions, trailing
zeros may be omitted.
Deﬁnition 2.2. Let λ and µ be partitions such that µ ⊆ λ. A tableau T of shape λ/µ
is said to be semistandard if

1. T (i, j) ≤ T (i, j + 1) if (i, j) and (i, j + 1) both lie in D(λ/µ), that is to say the

entries in each row are weakly increasing from left to right, and

2. T (i, j) < T (i + 1, j) if (i, j) and (i + 1, j) both lie in D(λ/µ), that is to say the

entries in each column are strictly increasing from top to bottom,

and is said to satisfy the lattice permutation property if

3. the number of entries s occurring in the ﬁrst r rows of T does not exceed the number

of entries s − 1 occurring in the ﬁrst r − 1 rows of T , for all r ≥ 1 and s ≥ 2.

It follows from the lattice permutation condition that the weight ν of T must be a

partition of length ℓ(ν) ≤ ℓ(λ) such that ν ⊆ λ and |ν| = |λ| − |µ|.

Deﬁnition 2.3. A tableau T is said to be a Littlewood–Richardson (LR) tableau if it is
semistandard and if it satisﬁes the lattice permutation property. We denote the set of all
LR tableaux of shape λ/µ and weight ν by LR(λ/µ, ν). This set will be empty unless
µ ⊆ λ, in which case ℓ(µ) ≤ ℓ(λ). For each positive integer n we denote the union of
those sets LR(λ/µ, ν) for which ℓ(λ) ≤ n by LR(n). We refer to elements of this set as
LR n-tableaux.

8

Example 2.4. In the case λ = (9, 9, 6, 4, 1), µ = (7, 5, 3) and ν = (7, 5, 2) a typical
tableau T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) ⊂ LR(5) ⊂ LR(6) ⊂ · · · takes the form:

1 1 2 2

where the empty cells occupy the shape speciﬁed by µ, and the cells with entries occupy
the shape speciﬁed by λ/µ, and the numbers of these entries for each integer k give the
parts νk of ν.

2.2 Hives, edge labels, gradients and interlocking Gelfand–Tsetlin

patterns

It is convenient to introduce a second combinatorial construct, namely, that of a hive ﬁrst
introduced by Knutson and Tao [KT99] with properties described in more detail by Buch
[Buc00].

A hive expresses itself in three diﬀerent forms. In its vertex representation, as given in
[KT99] and [Buc00], a hive is a labelling of the vertices of a planar, equilateral triangular
graph, as shown below on the left of (2.3), satisfying the rhombus inequalities indicated
on the right of (2.3).
If the side length of the hive is n, then the hive is called an n-
hive, and the edges connecting all neighbouring vertices divide the entire triangle into n2
elementary triangles of side length 1. An elementary rhombus is the union of any two
elementary triangles sharing a common edge. The rhombus inequalities require that for
each such rhombus the sum of the labels of the vertices at the ends of the common edge
must be greater than or equal to the sum of the labels of the other two vertices of the
rhombus. The hive is said to be an integer hive if all its vertex labels are integers.

A naming convention for the edge labels has been chosen so that αij, βij and γij label
the edges parallel to the left, right and lower boundaries, respectively, of the hive, with the
vertex labelled aij in (2.3) at their rightmost end. As a matter of convention we sometimes
refer to any edge parallel to the left, right or lower boundary as being an α-edge, β-edge
or γ-edge, respectively. The triangle conditions require that the edge labels α, β and γ

With this convention, the transformation from the vertex labelling to the edge labelling
is done by setting each edge label equal to the label of the edge’s right hand vertex minus
that of its left hand vertex, that is by setting:

αij = aij − ai,j−1 ;

βij = aij − ai−1,j ;

γij = aij − ai−1,j−1 .

(2.10)

(2.6) ensures that inductively ﬁnding the vertex labels from the edge labels starting with
a00 = 0 gives a consistent result.

Under this correspondence between vertex and edge representations of a hive, the

The parameters Rij, Uij and Lij introduced in these diagrams are not edge labels. They
are referred to as the gradients of the corresponding right-leaning, upright and left-leaning
rhombi, respectively. Each gradient is deﬁned to be the diﬀerence between parallel edge
labels in the relevant rhombus or, equivalently, the four-term alternating sign sum of the
vertex labels of that rhombus. To be precise, in terms of the edge labels the gradients are
given by:

Rij = αi−1,j−1 − αij = γi,j−1 − γij ;
Uij = αij − αi−1,j = βij − βi,j−1 ;
Lij = βi,j−1 − βi+1,j = γij − γi+1,j .

(2.13)

From the edge representation, the equalities on the right are a consequence of the triangle
conditions. The rhombus inequalities of (2.3) then take the form:

Rij ≥ 0, Uij ≥ 0, Lij ≥ 0 for all i, j such that 1 ≤ i < j ≤ n.

(2.14)

They are equivalent to the requirement that in each of the above diagrams (2.12) the label
on a thick edge is greater than or equal to the label on its parallel thin edge. Combining
the rhombi of (2.12) in pairs produces the trapezia of (2.9) and leads immediately to the
betweenness conditions (2.8).

All this gives rise to a third way of specifying hives, namely the gradient representation,
which involves labelling its boundary edges and giving the gradients of one or other of its
three sets of right-leaning, upright or left-leaning elementary rhombi. This is illustrated

Deﬁnition 2.7. An integer hive with a00 = 0 is said to be a Littlewood–Richardson
(LR) hive if all of its edge labels are non-negative integers. By virtue of the betweenness
conditions (2.8) this is equivalent to the requirement that its boundary edges are labelled
by the parts of partitions. For a ﬁxed positive integer n let H(n) denote the set of all
LR n-hives and let H(n)(λ, µ, ν) be the set of LR n-hives whose lower, left and right
boundary edge labels are speciﬁed by the parts of the partitions λ, µ and ν of lengths
ℓ(λ), ℓ(µ), ℓ(ν) ≤ n, arranged as exempliﬁed in the case n = 5 by

Since all edge labels are ≥ 0 it also follows that in every elementary triangle labelled as
in (2.6) we have both γ ≥ α and γ ≥ β. Moreover, repeated use of the triangle condition
(2.6) ensures that

|λ| = |µ| + |ν| .

(2.19)

hive H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) the non-negative integer α, β and γ edge labels automatically
specify three diﬀerently oriented interlocking Gelfand–Tsetlin (GT) patterns [GT50] of
types speciﬁed by the boundary edge labels µ, ν and λ, respectively.

2.3 Connection between hives and tableaux

Let n be a ﬁxed positive integer. Then there is a one-to-one correspondence between
LR n-tableaux and LR n-hives. Let f (n) denote the map from LR(n) to H(n). taking
T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) to H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) by setting the upright rhombus gradients of H to

Uij = #entries i in row j of T for 1 ≤ i < j ≤ n ,

(2.25)

and adding the boundary edge labels λ, µ and ν.

of entries i in row j of T . For i from 1 to j − 1 these are arranged in weakly increasing

for all (i, j) such that 1 ≤ i < j ≤ n. It will be recognised that the conditions Uij ≥ 0
correspond to the ﬁlling of the rows of T with non-negative numbers of integer entries,
while the conditions Rij ≥ 0 correspond to the fact that entries of T are strictly increasing
down columns, while the conditions Lij ≥ 0 correspond to the lattice permutation rule of
Deﬁnition 2.2 for 2 ≤ s ≤ r ≤ n, with all other cases trivial.

This suﬃces to show that the above map f (n) that takes T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) to H ∈
H(n)(λ, µ, ν), for any ﬁxed n ≥ ℓ(λ), is a bijection. Since this is true for all λ, µ, ν with
ℓ(λ) ≤ n, f (n) provides a bijection from LR(n) to H(n).

In order to interpret hive edge labels and the constituent Gelfand-Tsetlin patterns in
terms of tableau parameters, it might be noted that under this bijection, the partition
(λ1, λ2, . . . , λi, αi,i+1, αi,i+2, . . . , αin) speciﬁes the shape of the subtableau of T consisting
of all entries ≤ i including 0 or empty boxes, for i ≥ 0, with this partition coinciding
with µ in the special case i = 0. On the other hand the partitions (β1j, β2j, . . . , βjj) for
j = 1, 2, . . . , n specify the weights of the ﬁrst j rows of T , with this partition coinciding
with ν in the special case j = n. More explicitly, αij is the number of entries ≤ i in row
j, βij is the total number of entries i in rows 1, 2, . . . , j and γij = αi−1,j + βij. It follows
that:

• G(α)
µ

is obtained from T ∈ LR(n) by recording the sequence of partitions giving the

shapes occupied by the entries < r in rows r, r + 1, . . . , n of T , for r = 1, 2, . . . , n.

• G(β)
ν

is obtained from T ∈ LR(n) by recording the sequence of partitions giving the

weights of the subtableau consisting of the ﬁrst r rows of T for r = n, . . . , 2, 1.

2.4 Combinatorial models for LR coeﬃcients

The Littlewood–Richardson rule states that the coeﬃcients appearing in the expansion
(1.1) of a product of Schur functions are given by

cλ
µν = #{T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν)}.

(2.30)

It follows from the bijection between Littlewood–Richardson n-tableaux and Littlewood–
Richardson n-hives for any ﬁxed n ≥ ℓ(λ) that

cλ
µν = #{H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν)}.

(2.31)

This result is due to Fulton, as reported in the Appendix to [Buc00].

with that given in (2.31). In what follows, rather than explicitly using GT patterns, we
use the hive model based on (2.31). In doing so it is worth commenting here on the case
ℓ(λ) = r with r < n for which we have λn = 0. The hive conditions then imply that
µn = νn = 0 and the upright rhombus gradients Uin in the nth diagonal of any n-hive
H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) with λn = 0 are all 0. We say in such a case that the nth diagonal of
H is empty. Let κn be an operator whose action is to restrict any n-hive H with empty
nth diagonal to an (n − 1)-hive ˜H consisting of the leftmost (n − 1) diagonals of H. This
map from H(n)(λ, µ, ν) with λn = 0 to H(n−1)(λ, µ, ν) generated by the application of
κn is necessarily a bijection since both H(n)(λ, µ, ν) and H(n−1)(λ, µ, ν) are in bijective
correspondence with LR(λ/µ, ν), with the two maps from T to H and from T to ˜H,
governed by (2.25) for appropriate values n and n − 1, respectively, ensuring consistency
with the map κn : H 7→ ˜H.

The operations κk for k = 1, 2, . . . , n are required in Sections 8 and 10 where we con-
struct a bijective map from H(n)(λ, µ, ν) to H(n)(λ, ν, µ) and prove its involutive property
when extended to map from H(n) to H(n).

3 The action of deletion operators on tableaux

Our task is to provide a bijective map from all T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) to all S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ),
and to show that this bijection is an involution, or, equivalently, to provide a map from
all H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) to all K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ), and to show it has the same properties. As
a ﬁrst step it is useful to deﬁne certain deletion operators whose action on T or H will
enable us to build up S or K.

First we deal with tableaux, for which the deletion operators were ﬁrst introduced
in [Aze99, Aze00], and later described in [PV10]. Their action can be speciﬁed by modi-
fying slightly a deletion procedure described by Sagan and Stanley [SS90].
Deﬁnition 3.1. For any given T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν), augmented by the inclusion of entries
0 in each cell of D(µ), and any corner cell (r, λr) of D(λ) the deletion operator δr,λr is
deﬁned to be such that:
(i) if T (r, λr) = r then the action of δr,λr on T is just to delete the cell (r, λr). In this case
a parameter called the terminating row number is deﬁned to be 0.
(ii) if 0 < T (r, λr) < r then the action of δr,λr on T is ﬁrst to delete the cell (r, λr) and
move its entry into row r −1 by replacing the rightmost entry strictly less than itself, with
that replaced entry in turn being bumped into row r − 2, again replacing the rightmost
entry strictly less than itself, and continuing in this way until an entry 0 is replaced by
some non-zero entry x, say. Note that this ﬁnal replacement occurs at some corner cell
(k, µk) of D(µ) since, if the original position of the entry x is (k + 1, j), the cell (k, j)
must have been empty (otherwise there would have been an entry T (k, j) < x in row k)
so that we have µk ≥ j > µk+1. In this case the terminating row number is deﬁned to be
k. The set of the cells where the changes are made deﬁnes a deletion path P in the form
of a vertical strip from the corner (r, λr) of D(λ) to a corner (k, µk) of D(µ) with one cell
P [s] in row s for each s = r, r − 1, . . . , k. The reason why the path forms a vertical strip,

19

in other words why the column numbers in the path weakly increase as the replacements
go up, is reviewed in the proof of Lemma 3.3 below.
(iii) if T (r, λr) = 0 then the action of δr,λr on T is just to remove the corner cell (r, λr) =
(r, µr) of D(λ) and D(µ). This cell constitutes a deletion path consisting of a single
cell with a corresponding terminating row number r. It follows that type (iii) could be
included as a special case of type (ii), although this time the removed entry 0 is not
replaced by any non-zero entry.

While cases (i) and (iii) are rather trivial it is worth illustrating an example of case

(ii):

It might be noted that it is the Littlewood–Richardson property together with the fact
that the contents of a deletion path are strictly decreasing as one works up the tableau
that leads in cases of type (ii) to the deletion path terminating in row k with k ≥ 1. In
contrast to this in cases of type (i), if one performed the deletion as in cases of type (ii)
by means of deletions in the sense of Sagan and Stanley [SS90] starting from a corner
cell (r, λr) of T containing the row number r, then, due to the Littlewood–Richardson
property, the process would continue until it bumped a non-zero entry, namely 1, out of
row 1, i.e. it would be external. We avoid this as in [Aze99, Aze00, PV10] by restricting
the deletion to be just that of the single corner cell, but it explains why, in this situation,
the terminating row number is deﬁned to be zero.

One consequence of this is that in cases of type (i), while the semistandard property
is automatically preserved under deletion, the same is not always true of the lattice
permutation property. However, if T has no entries greater than r, including the case
ℓ(λ) = r, then it is easy to see that the type (i) deletion of the corner cell (r, λr) does
automatically preserve both the semistandard and the lattice permutation properties.
Similarly, it is easy to see rather trivially that both the semistandard and the lattice
permutation properties are preserved under deletions of type (iii). Moreover the deletion
procedure has been designed so that it also preserves both the semistandard and the
lattice permutation properties of tableaux under deletions of type (ii) as shown in the
following two lemmas.
Lemma 3.3. Let the partition λ have length ℓ(λ) ≤ n and let T be a semistandard
tableau of shape λ/µ for some µ ⊆ λ. If (r, λr) is a corner cell of D(λ) for which the

20

entry in T is strictly less than its row number r, then the action of δr,λr on T preserves
the semistandard property.
Proof: Let P be the deletion path corresponding to the action of δr,λr on T . Since
T (r, λr) < r, then as we saw earlier P terminates in some corner (k, µk) of D(µ). Let
cr > cr−1 > · · · > ck+1 > (ck =)0 be the entries of P in T . Removing the corner entry cr
from row r clearly does not aﬀect the semistandard property. For i = r, r −1, . . . , k +1 the
bumping process involves the rightmost entry ci from row i and say column j replacing the
rightmost entry ci−1 < ci in row i − 1 and say column l. This ensures that entries remain
weakly increasing from left to right across the row i − 1. Moreover the column strictness
of entries in T ensures that l ≥ j (this is why the deletion path forms a vertical strip). If
l = j the column strictness between rows i − 1 and i is preserved since after deletion ci
will lie immediately above either an empty site (outside the outer shape) if i = r or the
larger entry ci+1 that has replaced it in row i if i < r. If l > j then after deletion ci will
lie in row i − 1 immediately above either an empty site (outside the outer shape) or an
entry that was originally to the right of the rightmost ci in row i and is therefore greater
than ci. Thus the column strictness between rows i − 1 and i is preserved in column l.
That in column j is easier since, while the entry of the cell (i − 1, j) remains unchanged,
that of the cell (i, j) is now larger than before (or empty outside the outer shape). Thus
semistandardness is preserved.
(cid:3)
Lemma 3.4. Let the partition λ have length ℓ(λ) ≤ n and let T be a Littlewood–
Richardson tableau of shape λ/µ for some µ ⊆ λ. If (r, λr) is a corner cell of D(λ) for
which the entry in T is strictly less than its row number r, then the action of δr,λr on T
preserves the lattice permutation property.
Proof: As usual for each (i, j) such that 1 ≤ i ≤ j ≤ n, we let Uij be the number of
entries i in row j of T and let βij be the number of entries i in rows 1 through j of T .
The lattice permutation inequalities for T take the form Lij = βi,j−1 − βi+1,j ≥ 0 for
1 ≤ i < j ≤ n.

Again let P be the deletion path corresponding to the action of δr,λr on T , and note that
since T (r, λr) < r, the path P terminates in a corner of D(µ). Let k be the terminating
row number and let cr > cr−1 > · · · > ck+1 > 0 be the entries of P in T . Then as a result
of the deletion operation βcr,r−1, βcr−1,r−2, . . . , βck+1,k each increase by one, and all other
βij stay the same. It follows that the inequality Lij = βi,j−1 − βi+1,j ≥ 0 in T may only
be lost if βi+1,j increases by 1 and βi,j−1 stays the same upon deletion. This implies that
along P an entry i + 1 is bumped from row j + 1 to row j where it either replaces an entry
0 or bumps an entry l from row j to row j − 1 with l < i. In both cases this can only
occur if j + 1 ≤ r ≤ n and Ui+1,j+1 = βi+1,j+1 − βi+1,j ≥ 1 and Uij = βij − βi,j−1 = 0. It
follows that Lij = βi,j−1 − βi+1,j = βij − βi+1,j ≥ 1 + βij − βi+1,j+1 ≥ 1, where in the ﬁnal
step use has been made of the fact that Li,j+1 = βij − βi+1,j+1 ≥ 0. Since Lij is therefore
≥ 1 before the deletion along P and the deletion can only diminish it by at most 1, the
condition Lij ≥ 0 is maintained after deletion. Thus the lattice permutation property is
preserved.
(cid:3)

These Lemmas 3.3 and 3.4 enable us to prove in the following Lemma 3.5 that the

21

deletion procedure preserves the Littlewood–Richardson nature of a tableau. In order to
specify changes of outer and inner shapes and weights we deﬁne ǫi ∈ Zn with ith entry
equal to 1 and all other entries 0, for 1 ≤ i ≤ n.
Lemma 3.5. Let ℓ(λ) = n. The deletion operator δn,λn on T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) maps T to
˜T , with ˜T ∈ LR((λ − ǫn)/µ, (ν − ǫn)) if νn > 0, and ˜T ∈ LR((λ − ǫn)/(µ − ǫk), ν) if νn = 0
and the terminating row number is k with 1 ≤ k ≤ n.
Proof:
If νn > 0 there is nothing to prove. The lattice permutation rule ensures that
there is at least one entry n in row n, and the operator δn,λn just removes the cell (n, λn)
with entry n from T . The resulting tableau ˜T is clearly in LR((λ − ǫn)/µ, (ν − ǫn)).

If νn = 0 and λn > µn so that T (n, λn) is non-zero and less than n, then δn,λn performs
the usual type (ii) deletion as described above along a deletion path P in T starting at the
corner cell (n, λn) of D(λ) and terminating in a corner (k, µk) of D(µ) with 1 ≤ k < n. It
follows from Lemmas 3.3 and 3.4 in the case r = n that the action of δn,λn preserves both
the semistandard and the lattice permutation property. Having emptied the cell (n, λn)
and ﬁlled the cell (k, µk) with a non-zero entry we have ˜T ∈ LR((λ − ǫn)/(µ − ǫk), ν) as
claimed.

Finally, if νn = 0 and λn = µn so that T (n, λn) = 0, then the cell (n, λn) is removed
both from the inner shape µ and the outer shape λ, so that k = n and again there is
nothing to prove.
(cid:3)

We also require

Deﬁnition 3.6. For any given T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) with ℓ(λ) = r the full r-deletion operator
δr is deﬁned by

δr := δr,1δr,2 · · · δr,λr

(3.2)

Its action on T is realised by acting with δr,λr ﬁrst and δr,1 last. In general the action
proceeds in three phases:
in Phase 1 corner cells are deleted in turn as in case (i) until
there remain no entries r in row r, and then Phase 2 proceeds by means of deletion
operations as in case (ii) until there are no non-zero entries in row r. Finally, Phase 3
corresponds to the removal of the empty cells in row r as in case (iii). If ℓ(λ) < r the full
r-deletion is understood to be a null-operator.

In this example we took the liberty of indicating the ﬁrst corner deletion and then
its successor path deletion on the same diagram. This is always possible since the cells
involved are quite distinct from one another. The same is true for any two successive
path deletions brought about by the action of δr,j and δr,j−1, as will be made clear in the
Horizontal Path Comparison Lemma below.

Before stating it, we provide some preliminaries. Let (r, λr) be a corner cell of D(λ)
and let P be the deletion path of δr,λr applicable to T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) with 0 < T (r, λr) < r
and terminating row number t (< r). Let λ♭ and µ♭ be deﬁned by D(λ♭) = D(λ)−{(r, λr)}
and D(µ♭) = D(µ) − {(t, µt)} respectively, so that δr,λr T has shape λ♭/µ♭, and set P ♭ =
P ∩ D(λ♭/µ♭) = P − {(r, λr)}. Then the bottom cell P ♭[r − 1] (resp. the top cell P ♭[t]) of
P ♭ is bounded at the bottom (resp. the top) by a horizontal segment of the outer border
(resp. the inner border) of D(λ♭/µ♭).
In general, for any skew LR tableau, a vertical
strip P ♭ ascending from a cell bounded at the bottom by a horizontal segment of its
outer border to a cell bounded at the top by a horizontal segment of its inner border,
and containing one cell of its shape from every row in between, determines as illustrated
below the following subsets of the shape of the tableau: that subset strictly to the left of
P ♭ which, by convention, also includes everything below the row where P ♭ starts, and that
subset weakly to the right of P ♭, that is the union of P ♭ itself and the subset strictly to
the right of P ♭ which, again by convention, also includes everything above the row where
P ♭ terminates. If P ♭ is obtained from a deletion path P by removing its starting cell and
if there would be no confusion, these subsets are also called strictly to the left of P and
so on.

In the example below on the left, the cell marked by

is the starting cell of P and
form P ♭. The plain white region shows the subset strictly to the left of
those marked by
P , the dots ﬁll the subset strictly to the right of P , which is joined by the cells
to form
the subset weakly to the right of P . Below on the right is an example of part of some
shape λ#/µ# containing the same P ♭ and whose bottom and top cells are the bottom and
top nonempty cells in their respective columns.

After a deletion along P starting from (r, λr), another deletion path P ′ could start
either from any corner strictly to the left of P or from one weakly to the right of P ,
according to which the entire new deletion path P ′ lies either strictly to the left of P or

23

weakly to the right of P , due to the type of argument used repeatedly since [Kn70, proof
of Theorem 1](Knuth) in dealing with insertions rather than deletions, see also [Sch63,
Remarque 2] (Sch¨utzenberger). We now include a proof of this claim not only for the sake
of maintaining a self-contained presentation but also to take this opportunity to generalise
it in a manner to be used later.
Lemma 3.8.
[Elementary Path Comparison] Let T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν), (r, λr) a corner of
D(λ) such that T (r, λr) < r and P the deletion path of δr,λr applied to T with terminating
row number t. Set T ′ = δr,λr T ∈ LR(λ♭/µ♭, ν) where λ♭ and µ♭ are deﬁned as above.
Moreover, let T # be an LR tableau of some shape λ#/µ# containing the same vertical
strip P ♭ = P − {(r, λr)} having the same entries as in T ′, with its bottom cell (resp.
top cell) bounded below (resp. above) by a horizontal segment of the outer (resp. inner)
border of D(λ#/µ#). Let (r′, λ#
r′) < r′ and P #
the deletion path of δr′,λ#

r′) be a corner of D(λ#) such that T #(r′, λ#
applied to T # with terminating row number t′.

(1) Suppose that (r′, λ#

r′) lies strictly to the left of P , and that the part of T # to the left
of P coincides that part of T ′ (and hence that part of T as well). Then the entire
P # runs strictly to the left of P , and we have t′ ≥ t.

(2) Suppose that (r′, λ#

r′ ) lies weakly to the right of P (in this case the part of T # strictly
to the left of P can be diﬀerent from that part of T ′ so long as the assumptions
made before part (1) are satisﬁed). Then the entire P # runs weakly to the right of
P , and we have t′ < t.

Remark 3.9. Note that the assumptions for T # in both cases (1) and (2) are satisﬁed by
the tableau T ′ = δr,λrT , so that both statements (1) and (2) are applicable, in particular,
to a deletion occurring immediately after δr,λr . In addition, we can (and will) apply (2)
after performing several deletions from T ′ so long as they do not change the contents of
P or stack any cells above the terminating cell of P (see the proof of the Vertical Path
Comparison Lemma 3.11 below).

Proof. As in Deﬁnition 3.1, let P [s] denote the cell of the path P in row s for any applicable
s. This notation will be used for other deletion paths as well.

The claim (1) is obviously true if P # terminates in or below row r, that is if t′ ≥ r.
So we consider the case where t′ < r. Note that P # lies strictly to the left of P in rows
r and lower since everything in such rows is considered to be strictly to the left of P ♭ by
convention. Now we proceed by decreasing induction on the row number i, with t ≤ i < r
and assume that P #, having run strictly to the left of P in rows > i, enters row i. Note
that, since i < r, the cell P [i + 1] exists, at least in T . Since P #[i + 1] lies strictly to
the left of P [i + 1] by the induction hypothesis, we have T #(P #[i + 1]) = T (P #[i + 1]) ≤
T (P [i + 1]) = T ′(P [i]) = T #(P [i]). This means that the candidate entries T #(P #[i]),
which must be < T #(P #[i + 1]), can only lie strictly to the left of P [i] in row i of T #, so
that P # proceeds strictly to the left of P [i], including the possibility of terminating there
with t′ = i ≥ t. If i = t, P # must terminate there since P [t] is the leftmost nonempty cell
in row t of T #. This inductively shows that the entire path P # runs strictly to the left
of P , and also that t′ ≥ t.

24

On the other hand, the claim (2) is obviously true if r′ < t, so we assume r′ ≥ t and
again proceed by decreasing induction on the row number i, with r′ ≥ i ≥ t. The initial
case i = r′ is covered by the assumption in (2) that P #[r′] is weakly to the right of P [r′].
If r′ > i ≥ t, we have T #(P #[i + 1]) ≥ T #(P [i + 1]) = T ′(P [i + 1]) > T ′(P [i]) with the
ﬁrst inequality following from the induction hypothesis on the location of P # in row i + 1,
and the next equality following from the equality of entries in T # and T ′ along P , while
the second inequality is the preserved evidence of the deletion along P . Thus the entry
T #(P #[i + 1]) goes to the cell P [i] or somewhere to its right, that is to say P #[i] lies
weakly to the right of P [i]. Since this continues up to i = t, in particular the deletion
δr′,λ#

applied to T # still bumps something from row t, we have t′ < t.

r′

In our setting where the starting cells of the deletions are taken ﬁrst from right to
left in row n, then from right to left in row n − 1 and so on, these considerations can be
usefully organized in the form of the following two lemmas, the ﬁrst of which is already
clear.
Lemma 3.10.
[Horizontal Path Comparison] Let T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) with ℓ(λ) = r. Con-
sider the full r-deletion operator δr = δr,1 · · · δr,λr applied to T . Let P and P ′ denote the
deletion paths of δr,j and δr,j−1 in this sequence, where 2 ≤ j ≤ λr − νr, and let t and t′
be their terminating row numbers. Then P ′ runs strictly to the left of P , and we have
t′ ≥ t.
Proof: This is immediate from part (1) of Lemma 3.8.

To establish this we use induction on j. First assume that j = 1. By the Horizontal

Comparison Lemma 3.10 applied to Pj−1 and Pj, Pj−1 lies strictly to the right of Pj, and

By the induction argument this shows, as required, that T #

4 Bijective map from LR(λ/µ, ν) to LR(λ/ν, µ)

With our deletion operators we are able to construct from any LR tableau T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν)
a partner LR tableau S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ). To do so we empty the initial LR tableau T by
performing a sequence of deletion operations, from right to left across each row in turn
from bottom to top. This renders it empty and simultaneously allows us to build the ﬁnal
LR tableau S from the data on the row number of the starting cell and the terminating
row number of each deletion path.

To accomplish this, we use a formulation in which the initial T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) and the
ﬁnal S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ) are associated with pairs (T (n), S(0)) and (T (0), S(n)), respectively,
where T (n) = T , S(n) = S and both S(0) and T (0) are empty tableaux, and we introduce
operators that modify both components of pairs.
Deﬁnition 4.1. For an LR tableau T with no more than n rows, set T (n) = T and
let S(0) be an empty tableau. Then let ∆(n) denote the operation which transforms the
pair (T (n), S(0)) into another pair ∆(n)(T (n), S(0)) := (T (0), S(n)) through the action of
a succession of n operators ∆n, ∆n−1, . . . , ∆1 that produce pairs (T (r), S(n−r)), with
r = n − 1, n − 2, . . . , 0, respectively, as indicated by

(T (n), S(0)) ∆n

7−→ (T (n−1), S(1))

∆n−1
7−→ · · · ∆2

7−→ (T (1), S(n−1)) ∆1

7−→ (T (0), S(n)),

(4.1)

where each ∆r maps (T (r), S(n−r)) to (T (r−1), S(n−r+1)) by applying the operation δr deﬁned
in Deﬁnition 3.1 to the left-hand component T (r), so that T (r−1) = δrT (r), and by placing
a new row on top of the right-hand component S(n−r) to produce S(n−r+1), with this row
consisting of cells containing the terminating row numbers of this δr operation, including
zeros, arranged from left to right across the new row in the order in which they are
identiﬁed under the action of δr. We call the tableau S(n) the partner tableau of T (n).

To see that the prescribed operations are applicable, note that, by repeated use of
Lemma 3.4, T (r) is an LR tableau with outer shape (λ1, . . . , λr). S(r) will be a certain
tableau of shape (λr+1, . . . , λn) with zero and positive entries, or in fact a ﬁlling of the
rows r+1 through n of the Young diagram D(λ) with such entries. In particular, T (0) is an
empty tableau, and S(n) is a ﬁlling of D(λ) with zero and positive entries. Furthermore,
we have

27

Theorem 4.2. Let n be a positive integer and let λ, µ and ν be partitions such that
ℓ(λ) ≤ n and µ, ν ⊆ λ with |λ| = |µ| + |ν|. For each LR tableau T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) let
T (n) = T and let S(0) be an empty tableau. If we let ∆(n)(T (n), S(0)) = (T (0), S(n)) as in
Deﬁnition 4.1, then S = S(n) is a LR tableau and S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ).

As a ﬁrst step towards proving this theorem we establish with the notation of Deﬁni-

tion 4.1 the following:
Lemma 4.3. Let µ(r) = (µ(r)
r ) denote the inner shape of T (r) for r = n, n −
1, . . . , 0. Then T (r) is an LR tableau of shape (λ1, . . . , λr)/µ(r) and weight (ν1, . . . , νr)
while S(n−r) is a tableau of shape (λr+1, . . . , λn)/(νr+1, . . . , νn) and weight µ − µ(r).

1 , . . . , µ(r)

Proof. The result is obvious for r = n in which case µ(n) = µ, T (n) has shape λ/µ and
S(0) is empty. So let us assume that the result has been veriﬁed for the pair (T (r), S(n−r))
for some 1 ≤ r ≤ n, and show the result for the pair (T (r−1), S(n−r+1)).

By Deﬁnition 4.1, we have T (r−1) = δrT (r). Recall from Deﬁnition 3.1 the three phases
of the action of δr which eventually shave oﬀ the whole row r from the outer shape of T (r).
Since, by the induction hypothesis, T (r) has no more than r rows and satisﬁes the lattice
permutation property, the entries r can only appear in row r. Moreover, the number of
such entries is νr since none can have been removed under the action of δs for s > r. Hence,
as described in Lemma 3.5, the operations in Phase 1 delete all νr entries r from T (r),
thereby reducing the weight from (ν1, . . . , νr) to (ν1, . . . , νr−1). The operations in Phases
2 and 3 do not remove any nonzero entry, so this weight remains unchanged. However,
in general they remove some cells from the inner shape of T (r), including all inner cells
in row r, making the new inner shape µ(r−1) (by our choice of notation). Since the LR
property is maintained, again by Lemma 3.5, it follows that T (r−1) is an LR tableau of
shape (λ1, . . . , λr−1)/µ(r−1) and weight (ν1, . . . , νr−1).

By Deﬁnition 4.1, S(n−r+1) is constructed by placing on top of S(n−r) a new row
containing the terminating row numbers of the deletions ocurring in the application of δr
to T (r). This row has length λr since δr consists of λr deletion operations. Phase 1 consists
of νr type (i) deletions each producing a terminating row number 0. Then Phases 2 and 3
produce λr − νr nonzero terminating row numbers of weight µ(r) − µ(r−1). It follows by the
induction argument that the weight of S(n−r+1) is (µ − µ(r)) + (µ(r) − µ(r−1)) = µ − µ(r−1),
as required.
(cid:3)

On the left, at each stage, the red boxes represent the terminating cells of the deletion

µ(r)/µ(r−1), while the green boxes represent the cells to be removed during Phase 1 of δr.
The tableaux T (r) are LR tableaux by Lemma 3.5. On the right, it may be noted that
the tableaux S(n−r) are all semistandard, and the ﬁnal one S(n) is an LR tableau. These

To complete the proof of Theorem 4.2 we require two additional Lemmas. First, using

the same notation as before:
Lemma 4.5. For r = 0, 1, . . . , n each tableau S(r) is semistandard.

Proof. Again we can proceed by induction. Since it contains no cells, the empty tableau
S(0) is semistandard. Then by the induction hypothesis S(n−r) is semistandard. However,

29

as we have indicated in the proof of Lemma 4.3, S(n−r+1) is constructed by placing on
top of S(n−r) a new row of length λr that contains on its left νr terminating row numbers
0, followed from left to right by λr − νr non-zero terminating row numbers. As required
for semistandardness these automatically appear in weakly increasing order thanks to the
Horizontal Path Comparison Lemma 3.10.

Since S(n−r) also satisﬁes the strict vertical inequalities by the induction hypothesis,
the only remaining condition required to establish the semistandardness of S(n−r+1) is the
vertical condition between its newly added row and the top row of S(n−r) (assuming that
r < n). We verify this by applying the Vertical Path Comparison Lemma 3.11 with its r
replaced here by r + 1. Phase 2 of δr+1 is divided into Phases 2A and 2B with U = Ur,r+1
being the number of Phase 2A deletions (i.e. the number of entries r in row r + 1 of
T (r+1)). By construction, the top row of S(n−r) and the new row of S(n−r+1) are as in the
following picture (see the statement of Lemma 3.11 for the notation m, tj and t#
j ). Note
that the lattice permutation property of T (r+1) implies that entries r can lie in rows r
and r + 1 only, so that the number of entries r in row r of T (r+1) is νr − U, and implies
furthermore that νr − U ≥ νr+1, or equivalently that νr+1 + U ≤ νr.

Remarkably, the map we have constructed from T = T (n) to S = S(n) is bijective. The

following lemma clariﬁes the basis of the proof of injectivity.
Lemma 4.7. Let n, λ, µ, ν, T and S be as in Theorem 4.2, and let (T (n), S(0)),
(T (n−1), S(1)), . . . , (T (0), S(n)) be as in Deﬁnition 4.1. Then, for each 1 ≤ r ≤ n, the
pair (T (r), S(n−r)) can be recovered from the sole knowledge of (T (r−1), S(n−r+1)) in the
following manner.

• S(n−r) consists of the lowermost n − r rows of S(n−r+1).

• Let Vkr denote the number of entries k in the top row of S(n−r+1) for 0 ≤ k ≤ r,
including entries 0 (in fact V0r equals νr, but we shall set aside this fact for the
moment). Then T (r) can be obtained from T (r−1) by applying Sagan and Stanley’s
internal insertion [SS90] from row k precisely Vkr times with k running from 1 up to
r in this order, and then adjoining V0r entries r to the right of the existing entries
in row r.

Proof. The assertion for S(n−r) is immediate from the construction. Now, again by con-
struction, the composition of the top row of S(n−r+1) tells that the full r-deletion applied
T (r), yielding T (r−1), consists of V0r type (i) deletions producing entries 0 in the top row
of S(n−r+1), then Vkr type (ii) deletions terminating in row k and producing that many
entries k in the top row of S(n−r+1), with k running from 1 up to r − 1 in this order, and
ﬁnally Vrr type (iii) deletions terminating in row r and producing that many entries r in
the top row of S(n−r+1). This ensures that it is possible to apply Sagan and Stanley’s
internal insertion to T (r−1) ﬁrst from each row k precisely Vkr times with k running from
r down to 1 in this order (note that the ﬁrst Vrr insertions from row r simply create that
many empty cells in row r), all reaching row r, and ﬁnally to adjoin V0r entries r to the

31

right of whatever already exists in row r, and that the result of these operations coincides
with T (r).
(cid:3)

Now the bijectivity is made explicit in the following:

Theorem 4.8. Let n, λ, µ and ν be as in Theorem 4.2, and let ρλ
LR(λ/µ, ν) to LR(λ/ν, µ) that takes T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) to S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ) by way of

µ,ν be the map from

T 7−→ (T, ∅) ∆(n)

7−→ (∅, S) 7−→ S .

(4.2)

Then ρλ

µ,ν is a bijection from LR(λ/µ, ν) to LR(λ/ν, µ).

Proof. Let T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) and let (T (r), S(n−r)), r = n, n − 1, . . . , 0, be as in Deﬁni-
tion 4.1. Now start with the pair (∅, S) = (T (0), S(n)) and apply Lemma 4.7 with r = n,
n − 1, . . . , 0 in this order to successively recover (T (1), S(n−1)), . . . , (T (n), S(0)) = (T, ∅).
Hence one can tell what T was by the sole knowledge of ρλ
µ,νT . This proves that the
map ρλ
µ,ν from LR(λ/µ, ν) to LR(λ/ν, µ) is injective, and hence we have the inequality
|LR(λ/µ, ν)| ≤ |LR(λ/ν, µ)|. Now a special feature of our situation is that we also have
a map ρλ
ν,µ going from LR(λ/ν, µ) to LR(λ/µ, ν) deﬁned in the same manner but with
the roles of µ and ν interchanged, since the construction works for any shape and weight
(the sizes have to match but that condition is symmetric in µ and ν), and by applying
the same argument to ρλ
ν,µ we also deduce |LR(λ/ν, µ)| ≤ |LR(λ/µ, ν)|. So these two sets
have the same cardinality, and it is ﬁnite. So any injection from one to the other is also
a surjection. Hence the map ρλ

µ,ν in the Theorem is a bijection.

Remark 4.9. With more eﬀort, it can be proved that the above recovering procedure
based on the internal insertion and adjunction described in Lemma 4.7 is applicable to
any S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ) and that it yields an element of LR(λ/µ, ν), without assuming that
S comes from a T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) via the map ρλ
µ,ν, for an illustrative example see [Aze99,
Aze00]. This allows one to prove the bijectivity without resorting to the ﬁniteness of
cardinalities. This strategy will be adopted in the language of LR hives in Section 9.

In the next section it will be proved that the collection of maps ρλ

µ,ν has an involutory
nature in the sense that the composition of maps ρλ
µ,ν is the identity. This means that
the inverse map from LR(λ/ν, µ) to LR(λ/µ, ν) mentioned in the previous paragraph,
based on internal insertion and adjunction, actually coincides with our original map ρλ
ν,µ
based on deletions. It follows that the insertion procedure of Lemma 4.7 provides another
method to obtain the map in the statement of Theorem 4.2.

5 Tableau proof of involutive nature of bijection

The aim of this section is to give a tableau-based proof of the involutive property of the
collection of bijections in the statement of Theorem 4.8. To be more precise, let ρ(n)
denote the map

ρ(n) : LR(n) → LR(n)

7−→ (∅, S) 7−→ S.

Theorem 5.1. The bijection ρ(n) is an involution.

The rest of this section constitutes a proof of Theorem 5.1 fulﬁlling in complete gen-

erality the strategy developed in [Aze00].

The case n = 1 is easy, since if ℓ(λ) ≤ 1, then both LR(λ/µ, ν) and LR(λ/ν, µ) consist
of single elements. Having this as the base case, we prove Theorem 5.1 by induction on n.
Let n ≥ 2 and T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) ⊆ LR(n), and set ∆(n)(T, ∅) = (∅, S), namely S = ρ(n)T .
It is convenient to let S− denote the tableau obtained from S by removing the nth row.
The inductive nature of the construction of ρ(n) is such that S− can be regarded as the
output of ρ(n−1) applied to δnT ∈ LR(n−1) since S− is just the rows 1, 2, . . . , n−1 of S. By
induction, we can assume that ρ(n−1)(S−) = δnT . Our goal is to show that ρ(n)(S) = T .
Since we do not do this yet, we write T ′ for ρ(n)(S) for the moment. Suppose we can
prove the following
Proposition 5.2. Let n ≥ 2 and S ∈ LR(n), and set T ′ = ρ(n)(S). Set T ′′ = ρ(n−1)(S−).
Then we have δnT ′ = T ′′. Namely the following diagram commutes:

Let λ, ν and µ be the outer shape, the inner shape and the weight of S respectively,
namely S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ). The validity of Proposition 5.2 means that both T and T ′ are
transformed into the same tableau T ′′ = ρ(n−1)(S−) by δn. Moreover, T and T ′ both have
shape λ/µ and weight ν. Hence, whether one starts with (T, ∅) or (T ′, ∅), the one-row
semistandard tableau created by ∆n on the right-hand side consists of νr empty cells
created in Phase 1 and, if we let µ(n−1) denote the inner shape of T ′′, exactly µk − µ(n−1)
entries k with k running from 1 up to n − 1 in this order, all created in Phase 2, and
ﬁnally µn entries n created in Phase 3; namely, if one denotes this one-row tableau by R,
we have ∆n(T, ∅) = ∆n(T ′, ∅) = (T ′′, R). By Lemma 4.7 we have T = T ′.
So the proof of Theorem 5.1 is now reduced to that of Proposition 5.2.

k

33

We ﬁrst deal with the special case where row n of S consists of exactly one cell, but
afterwards we will argue that the general case can be reduced to this special case. We
start with some particularly easy special cases.
Lemma 5.3. If λn = 1 and the cell (n, 1) is empty in S, then Proposition 5.2 holds.

Proof. Consider applying ∆(n) to S to produce T ′, as in Deﬁnition 4.1. In applying ∆n,
the only deletion δn,1 is of type (iii), and produces a single cell containing n on the right-
hand component, where the word right (and left also, which will appear presently) is
chosen in accordance with the computation of ρ(n)(S), i.e. the pairs (S, ∅) and (∅, T ′).
Hence the left-hand component at this stage, δnS, coincides with S−, and applying δn−1,
. . . , δ1 to it produces the same rows on the right-hand component as does applying ∆(n−1)
to (S−, ∅). This means that T ′ consists of T ′′ = ρ(n−1)(S−) plus one cell (n, 1) containing
n. Applying δn to T ′ incurs a Phase 1 deletion only, which removes the cell (n, 1) and T ′′
is what is left. Hence Proposition 5.2 holds in this case.
(cid:3)

Lemma 5.4. If λn = 1 and S(n, 1) = n, then Proposition 5.2 holds.

Proof. Consider applying ∆(n) to S to produce T ′, as in Deﬁnition 4.1. In applying ∆n,
only Phase 1 arises, removing the cell (n, 1) and producing a single empty cell on the
right-hand component. Aside from this diﬀerence, the situation is very much similar to
the preceding case, and the validity of Proposition 5.2 in this case follows by the same
argument.
(cid:3)

We treat one more case separately, which not only is a special case but also serves as
the terminating step of an iteration occurring in a more general case to be discussed next.
Lemma 5.5. If λn = 1 and S(n, 1) = n − 1, then Proposition 5.2 holds.

Proof. In once again applying ∆(n) to S to produce T ′ as in Deﬁnition 4.1, the operation
δn applied to S consists of a single operation δn,1. It brings the bottom entry n − 1 to
row n − 1, where it replaces the rightmost entry < n − 1 in that row, which we call y
and, unless y = 0, incurs further bumping. Let P0 denote this deletion path, and l its
terminating row number. In applying δn−1 to δnS, after the removal of the entries n − 1 in
Phase 1, the Phase 2 and 3 deletions start from the cells in row n − 1 strictly to the left of
P0[n − 1] (see Deﬁnition 3.1 (ii) for this notation). Let P1, P2, . . . , Pm (m = λn−1 − µn−1)
denote these deletion paths, and k1, k2, . . . , km their terminating row numbers. The
bottom two rows of T ′ produced by these operations are as follows.

Proof of Lemma 5.6. Set e = S(n, 1) as in the statement of Proposition 5.8. If n − e = 1,
then Lemma 5.5 readily shows the validity of Proposition 5.2 in this case. So let us assume
that n − e ≥ 2. In this case we can apply Proposition 5.8. What it says can be read as
follows. If one applies ρ(n) to S, then δn,1 yields the terminating row number l′ and then
δn−1 yields the row R′ of terminating row numbers; these form 2{T ′, and the remaining
operations yield n−2{T ′, where the notation of part (1) of Deﬁnition 5.7 has been used.
Note that this whole procedure can also be described as applying δ1 · · · δn−2 · δn−1 · ˆδn,1
to S−. We compare this with applying the following operations to S: ﬁrst temporarily
remove the entry e at (n, 1), apply δn−1, and then adjoin e back to (n − 1, 1) and apply
δ1 · · · δn−2δn−1; in other words applying δ1 · · · δn−2 · ˆδn−1,1 ·δn−1 to S−. Then δn−1 yields the
row R′′ of terminating row numbers, ˆδn−1,1 yields l′′ by part (2) of Proposition 5.8, and
since ˆδn−1,1δn−1S− = δn−1ˆδn,1S− by part (1) of Proposition 5.8, the operations δ1 · · · δn−2
again yield n−2{T ′. This is illustrated in the middle picture of (5.2) below; note that this
three part middle picture is not meant to be a tableau, although the adjunction of l′′ to
n−2{T ′ at (n − 1, 1) is in fact an LR tableau (this follows easily from the LR property of
T ′ and the fact that l′′ was one of the entries in row n − 1 of T ′), which we temporarily
denote by T ∗.

Lemma 5.11. Let S be as in Proposition 5.2, and assume that λn > 0. Let S← denote
the tableau obtained from S by erasing the cell (n, 1) and shifting each of the remaining
cells in row n to the left by one column. Note that S← ∈ LR(n). Then we have ρ(n)S← =
δn,λnρ(n)S.

of the partner 4 for S by a single deletion δn,λn, starting with the entry denoted by x in
the drawing. Note that row n of 4 is drawn detached from the rest of the tableau. This
is to show an arrow connecting the cell (n, λn) to row n − 1. As we shall see towards the

Next we compare the partners 4 for S and 3 for S′. Note that S′ appears in the

42

process of applying ∆n to (S, ∅) as a left-hand side component. At that point, the cells
(n, 1) through (n, λn − 1) on the right-hand side component, namely those cells of the
partner 4 , have been constructed by placing the terminating row numbers of the red
wavy paths shown in 1 , indicated by i, j and k in the drawing.
If we continue, the
remaining deletion δn,1 applied to S′ produces a terminating row number, denoted by x
in the drawing, to be placed at (n, λn) of 4 , and then δn−1, . . . , δ1 produce rows n − 1
through 1 of 4 . If we start from S′ to produce 3 instead, the transformations of the
left-hand side components are exactly the same, but the terminating row number of the
ﬁrst deletion δn,1 for S′, which is also x, is placed at (n, 1), and the rows n−1 through 1 of
3 , produced by the continuing deletions δn−1, . . . , δ1, are exactly the same as those of 4
as indicated by the = sign in the drawing. Thus, the tableau 4 can be described as being
obtained from 3 by moving the cell (n, 1) to (n, λn) and placing the terminating row
numbers of the possibly symbolic red wavy lines in 1 in cells (n, 1) through (n, λn − 1).
Similarly, or even more simply, the partner 8 for S← can be described as the partner 7
for 6 plus a row consisting of the terminating row numbers of the red wavy lines shown
in 5 , attached as row n. Note again that these terminating row numbers, indicated by
i, j and k in the drawing, are exactly the same as those of the red wavy lines shown in
1 , in other words the contents of the cells (n, 1) through (n, λn − 1) of 4 .

Since the topmost n − 1 rows of 4 are exactly the same as those of 3 , the ﬁrst
deletion δn,λn for 4 and the ﬁrst deletion δn,1 for 3 , both starting with the entry denoted
by x in the drawing and hence belonging to the same type, follow the same path, if they
are of type (ii), and incur the same changes (no changes in the case of type (i) or (iii))
in the topmost n − 1 rows of 4 and 3 respectively. This claim in the case of type (ii)
is veriﬁed by consulting Deﬁnition 3.1, according to which the deletion path proceeds
iteratively from the current cell by looking for the rightmost cell in the row immediately
above which contains an entry stricty smaller than that of the current cell, which ensures
that the deletion path is determined by the value of the starting entry and the tableau
above the starting row, regardless of the column position of the starting entry. However,
at ﬁrst sight it may sound suspicious to claim that the path obtained from the blue wavy
line in 3 by moving the starting cell to the right still constitutes the beginning of a valid
deletion path if one worries about the possibility of moving the entry x in 3 too far to the
right to form a vertical strip. The point is that how far x is moved to the right is bounded
by the semistandardness of the tableau 4 that is itself constructed as the partner of the
originally given tableau S. Then as reviewed in the proof of Lemma 3.3 this automatically
assures that the path generated according to Deﬁnition 3.1 is a vertical strip.
(cid:3)

Now we can state the proof of Proposition 5.2, thereby concluding the proof of Theo-

rem 5.1.

Proof of Proposition 5.2. If λn = 0, then S and S− coincide, and T ′ and δnT ′ coincide,
so the conclusion is immediate. If λn > 0, then we can apply Lemma 5.11 successively to
S, S←, (S←)← and so on, to show that ρ(n−1)S− is obtained from ρ(n)S by applying δn,λn,
δn,λn−1, δn,λn−2 and so on, namely by applying δn, as required.
(cid:3)

Lemmas 6.1, 6.2 and 6.10 (plus some narrative text augmenting some minor points), and
the proof of Lemma 6.2 has a similar structure, and so on.

smaller than l′. Hence we have l′′ > 0.

7 Path removals from a hive

By virtue of the bijection between Littlewood–Richardson tableaux and hives, the action
of deletion operators on tableaux corresponds to a path removal procedure in hives. Under
this bijection the three types of action (i), (ii) and (iii) of the deletion operator δr,λr on
an LR tableau T with a corner cell at (r, λr) as described in Deﬁnition 3.1 correspond to
three types of hive path removal that we now deﬁne.

It should be recalled ﬁrst that in any given hive a diagonal consists of all the triangles
in a strip parallel to the right hand boundary of the hive lying between an edge on the
base and a corresponding edge on the left hand boundary. Our convention is that the pth
diagonal is the one bounded by the edges λp and µp. Then by a path we mean a connected
set of edges extending from an edge on the base to an edge on either the left or right hand
boundary. Such paths are generally zig-zag in nature and proceed either up a diagonal or
horizontally leftwards from one diagonal to another. They consist of pairs of edges taken
from a sequence of neighbouring triangles always including their common edge.
Deﬁnition 7.1. For any given H ∈ H(r)(λ, µ, ν) with r = ℓ(λ) we may deﬁne three path
removal operators χr, φr and ωr whose action on H is to reduce or increase edge labels
by 1 along a path starting from the edge labelled λr on the base of the hive and speciﬁed
as follows:

(i) χr: if νr > 0 then the path consists of the edges labelled λr and νr, with both edge

labels decreased by 1;

(ii) φr: if λr − µr − νr > 0 so that Uir > 0 for some i < r then the path proceeds up
the rth diagonal from the edge labelled λr through upright rhombi of gradient 0
until it encounters an upright rhombus of positive gradient, at which point it moves
horizontally to the left into the (r − 1)th diagonal and proceeds up this diagonal
or to the left as before, and so on until it terminates on the left-hand boundary at
the top of the kth diagonal, that is at the edge labelled µk for some k such that
1 ≤ k < r, with all path α and γ edge labels being decreased by 1 and all path β
edge labels increased by 1;

(iii) ωr: if µr > 0 then the path proceeds directly up the rth diagonal until it terminates
on the left-hand boundary at level r, that is at the edge labelled µr, with all path
edge labels decreased by 1.

The three types of path are illustrated below. In each case the action of χr, φr and
ωr on the hive H is to decrease the label of each red edge by 1 and to increase that of
each blue edge by 1. In particular, under this action the edge label λr and one or other

55

of νr, µk (with k < r) or µr are each reduced by 1 to λr − 1 and νr − 1, µk − 1 or µr − 1,
respectively.

(ii) φr:

(7.1)
While the structure of the paths is rather simple in cases (i) and (iii), the structure of
the path in case (ii) is more complicated and consists of a sequence of ladders in each
diagonal from the rth to the kth. Each ladder consists of a continuous zig-zag of red α- or
γ-edges passing through upright rhombi of gradient 0 that extends up the diagonal from
an edge that is either on the base of the hive or the top of an upright rhombus that we call
the foot rhombus (coloured green), to an edge that is either on the left hand boundary or
the bottom of an upright rhombus that we call the head rhombus (coloured yellow), as
illustrated below:

head rhombus

ladder

>0

0

0

0

0

foot rhombus

(7.2)

The length of a ladder is the number of its rungs, that is to say the number of horizontal
red γ-edges. Thus the ladders starting at the base of the hive lack a foot rhombus, while
those ending on the left hand boundary lack a head rhombus. It might be noted that all
ladders, including these, may consist of a single red α-edge, in which case the length is
0. The passage between one diagonal and the next is by way of a blue β-edge parallel to
the right hand boundary. If such an edge is the lth from the top of the diagonal then its
level is said to be l.

Before using these path removals we ﬁrst establish that the action of each of the path

removal operators preserves the hive conditions, as follows:
Lemma 7.2. Let H be a hive in H(r)(λ, µ, ν) with r = ℓ(λ). Then the path removal
operators are such that if we set ˜H = χr H, φr H or ωr H with νr > 0, λr − µr − νr > 0
and µr > 0, respectively, then in each case ˜H is a hive.
Proof:

It is easy to see that the triangle conditions (2.6) are preserved under any of the

56

edge labels of elementary triangles as illustrated by:

Moreover all edge labels remain non-negative under the map from H to ˜H. This is
obvious in the case of all blue edge labels since they are increased by 1 in the passage from
H to ˜H. On the other hand red edge labels are each decreased by 1, so it is necessary to
show that each red edge label in H is positive.

This is clear in case (i) since the only two red edges are those labelled λr and νr, which
by hypothesis are positive. In case (ii) the fact that λr > 0 implies that all γ-edges in the
ﬁrst r diagonals of H carry positive labels, this includes all the red γ-edges labels. This
only leaves the red α-edges. On any given pth diagonal these are separated by upright
rhombi of gradient 0, so they all carry the same label. For p > k this label must be positive
since at the top of each the ladder there appears a head rhombus of positive gradient. In
the kth diagonal the shared red α-edge label is µk, and the fact that µk is also positive
follows from the application of the betweenness condition to the conﬁguration

> 0

µk

(7.4)

In case (iii) the hive conditions imply that the labels of all red γ-edges are ≥ λr and those
of all red α-edges are ≥ µr. These red edge labels must therefore all be positive since
by hypothesis λr > 0 and µr > 0. Thus all red edge labels of H are positive in all three
cases, and as a consequence all edge labels of ˜H are non-negative integers.

Turning next to rhombi, as a result of the path removals the requisite changes in red
and blue edge labels automatically change the gradients of certain elementary rhombi.
In order that ˜H be an LR hive it is necessary to show that all these gradients remain
non-negative.
