upright rhombi speciﬁed in the diagram as 0 and > 0 ensure that β′ = β′′ and β′′′ > β.
In addition the gradient β′′ − β′′′ of the upper left-leaning rhombus must be non-negative.
Hence β′ = β′′ ≥ β′′′ > β. It follows that L = β′ − β > 0 so that L − 1 ≥ 0. This conﬁrms
that all rhombus gradients remain non-negative under the action of φr.

Finally, for a hive path removal of type (iii) as illustrated on the right of (7.1) the only

rhombi whose gradients change are those undergoing the map:

of type (i); Phase 2 consists of λr − νr − µr hive path removals of type (ii); and Phase 3
consists of µr hive path removals of type (iii), so that

Theorem 7.6. For a hive H ∈ H(r)(λ, µ, ν) with ℓ(λ) ≤ r let θrH = ˜H then ˜H ∈
H(r)(˜λ, ˜µ, ˜ν) with ˜λ = (λ1, . . . , λr−1, 0), ˜µ = (µ1 − V1r, . . . , µr−1 − Vr−1,r, 0) and ˜ν =
(ν1, . . . , νr−1, 0), where Vkr is the number of type (ii) hive path removals from H that ex-
tend from the boundary edge initially labelled λr to that initially labelled µk for 1 ≤ k < r.
Proof:
If ℓ(λ) < r then λr = µr = νr = 0 and there are no path removals so that Vkr = 0
for all k = 1, 2, . . . , r − 1. This implies that ˜λ = λ, ˜µ = µ, ˜ν = ν and ˜H = θrH = H ∈
H(r)(˜λ, ˜µ, ˜ν), as required. For ℓ(λ) = r the required result is an easy consequence of the
iterated use of Lemma 7.4. The edge label νr is reduced to 0 in Phase 1. At the same time
the edge label λr is reduced to λ −νr in Phase 1, then to λ −νr −µr in Phase 2, and ﬁnally
to 0 in Phase 3, in which the edge label µr is also reduced to 0. Meanwhile in Phase 2 the
parameters Vkr give the number of hive removal paths that reach the left-hand boundary
edge initiall labelled µk thereby reducing this label to µk − Vkr for k = 1, 2, . . . , r − 1. (cid:3)

Two further observations are of use in what follows.

Lemma 7.7. In the action of θr on H ∈ H(r)(λ, µ, ν) with ℓ(λ) = r if a hive path removal
of type (ii) follows a path P and reaches the left-hand boundary at level k, then the next
such removal follows a path P ′ lying weakly above the path P in each diagonal and reaches
level k′ with k′ ≥ k.
Proof: This can be seen by consideration of the following hive path removal diagram
in which two successive removal paths P and P ′ are illustrated, P in red, P ′ in blue and

60

(7.16)

The interpretation of this is that the ﬁrst path P is the union of the red and magenta
edges, while the second path P ′ is the union of the blue and magenta edges. Working
from right to left the route of P ′ necessarily coincides with that of P , as shown in the initial
magentasection, unless and until it reaches an upright rhombus, indicated in purple,
whose initial gradient 1 was reduced to 0 by the removal of the path P . At this point the
paths separate but they may meet again, whereupon the path P ′ again must coincide with
that of P , as shown in the second magenta section, unless and until it reaches another
such upright rhombus, again indicated in purple, whose initial gradient 1 was reduced
to 0 by the removal of the path P . In such a case the paths separate again, and so on.
This observation guarantees that the path P ′ lies weakly above that of P and also that
it meets the left-hand boundary at an edge weakly above that reached by P .
(cid:3)
Corollary 7.8. Under the action of θr on H ∈ H(r)(λ, µ, ν) with ℓ(λ) = r let Vkr be
the number of type (ii) hive path removals reaching the left-hand boundary at level k for
1 ≤ k < r. Then for each such k

µk ≥ µk − Vkr ≥ µk+1.

(7.17)

Proof:
The ﬁrst inequality follows from the fact that Vkr ≥ 0. Then, by virtue of
Lemma 7.7, the left-hand boundary edge label µk+1 must remain unchanged under all Vkr
hive path removals reaching the edge labelled µk. Each one of these decreases this label by
1. The fact that the hive conditions are preserved under these hive path removals implies
that on the left-hand boundary the kth edge label must remain greater than or equal to
that of the (k + 1)th edge label. Thus after all Vkr path removals we have µk − Vkr ≥ µk+1,
that is the second inequality of (7.17).
(cid:3)

respectively. Then

8 Path removal map from H(n)(λ, µ, ν) to H(n)(λ, ν, µ)

Armed with our path removal procedures we are able to exploit them to construct from
any hive H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) a partner hive K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ). To do so it is only necessary
to evacuate the initial hive H by performing a sequence of path removals that render
it empty and to build the ﬁnal hive K from the data on the location of the ﬁrst and
last edges on each path that is removed. In doing so one constructs a sequence of pairs
(H (r), K (n−r)) for each r = n, n − 1, . . . , 0 where H (r) is an r-hive and K (n−r) is an r-
truncated n-hive consisting of the rightmost n − r diagonals of some n-hive. These pairs
are such that H (n) = H and H (0) is the empty hive, signiﬁed here by a single point, while
K (n) = K and K (0) is an empty n-truncated n-hive, signiﬁed by a single boundary line
consisting of β-edges with labels µ1, µ2, . . . , µr.

Theorem 8.4. Let n be a positive integer and let λ, µ and ν be partitions such that
ℓ(λ) ≤ n and µ, ν ⊆ λ with |λ| = |µ|+|ν|. For each LR hive H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) let H (n) = H
and let K (0) be an n-truncated n-hive with edge labels µ. If we let Θ(n)(H (n), K (0)) =
(H (0), K (n)) as in Deﬁnition 8.2, then H (0) is an empty hive and K = K (n) is an LR hive
K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ). In such a case we write K = σ(n)H.
Proof:
From the iteration scheme of Deﬁnition 8.2 and the illustration of the action
of Θr in mapping (8.2) to (8.3) it is clear that K = K (n) will have boundary edge labels
λ, ν and µ.
In addition it can be seen that each Phase of the action of Θr preserves
the triangle condition at every stage. The possibility of an edge label becoming negative
arises only in the case of a Phase 2 type (ii) hive path removal as illustrated by the red
edge in (8.6). However, arguing inductively, the edge labels µ(n)
k = µk are non-negative
for 1 ≤ k < n. If, by the induction hypothesis the edge labels µ(r)

69

9 Creation of a hive by path additions

Having used a path removal procedure to provide a map from any H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) to
some K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ) we now wish to show that a path addition procedure may be used
to provide a map from any K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ) to some H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν). The aim is to
show that these two maps are mutually inverse to one another, thereby proving that each
is a bijection.

Our approach is to move successively from an (r − 1)-hive H (r−1) to an r-hive H (r)
In doing so it is necessary to
under a procedure dictated by the rth diagonal of K.
exploit ﬁrst the operator κ−1
that adds to H (r−1) an rth diagonal consisting of a sequence
of upright rhombi all of gradient 0, with its upper and lower boundary edge labels 0, and
with its remaining new edges given the unique labels that preserve the hive conditions.
This is the inverse of the bijection κr introduced and exempliﬁed in the case r = 4 at the
end of section 2.

r

To proceed further, let UH(r)(λ, µ, ν) be the set of all triangular arrays of side length
r satisfying the hive triangle relations and the condition that all upright rhombus labels
are non-negative integers, with boundary edges labelled by λ, µ and ν. For each H ∈
UH(r)(λ, µ, ν) we deﬁne path addition operators χr, φk and ωr analogous to the path
removal operators χr, φr and ωr of section 7.
Deﬁnition 9.1. For any given H ∈ UH(r)(λ, µ, ν) with ℓ(λ) ≤ r we deﬁne three path
addition operators χr, φk and ωr whose action on H is to increase or reduce edge labels
by 1 along paths speciﬁed as follows:

(i) χr: the path consists of the boundary edges labelled νr and λr, with each of these

labels being increased by 1;

(ii) φk: for any k < r the path proceeds down the kth diagonal from the edge labelled
µk through upright rhombi of gradient 0 until it encounters an upright rhombus of
positive gradient, at which point it moves horizontally to the right into the (k + 1)th
diagonal and proceeds down this diagonal or to the right as before, and so on until
it either reaches the base of the hive and then moves to the right or reaches the
right hand boundary and then moves down the rth diagonal until, in both cases, it
terminates at the edge labelled λr, with all path α and γ edge labels being increased
by 1 and all path β edge labels decreased by 1 ;

(iii) ωr: the path proceeds directly down the rth diagonal until it terminates at the base

at the edge labelled λr, with all path edge labels increased by 1.

These three types of path addition are illustrated below. In each case every

bfblue edge label is increased by 1 and every red edge label is decreased by 1. In the case
of χr and ωr the path additions are conﬁned to the rightmost rth diagonal. On the other
hand the path addition route ascribed to the action of φk with 1 ≤ k < r consists of a
sequence of ladders through upright rhombi of gradient 0 in each diagonal from the kth
to the rth, with the passage from each diagonal to the next taking place through a red

70

β-edge.

Each ladder (coloured blue) extends down its diagonal either from the top left hand
boundary or from an upright rhombus (coloured green) until it either reaches an upright
rhombus of positive gradient (coloured yellow) or the base of the triangular array. Thus,
the ladders starting on the left hand boundary lack a head rhombus, while those ending
at the base lack a foot rhombus. As shown in the above example of type (ii), ladders
may consist of a single blue edge.
In such a case the ladder is said to have length 0.
More generally, its length is the number of upright rhombi it crosses, that is to say the
number of its horizontal blue edges. The path addition is such that the gradient of the
foot rhombus is decreased by 1 from its initial positive value, while the gradient of the
head rhombus is increased by 1.

It might be noted here that there are two distinct manners in which type (ii) paths
may terminate. They are illustrated below, with the ﬁgures on the left and right applying
to cases in which the path addition reaches the base in the diagonal with j = r and j < r,
respectively.

illustrated immediately after any one of the actions of φk. Prior to this action the edges
labelled α, α0 and α1 had labels α − 1, α0 − 1 and α1, respectively. For the addition path
to enter the dth diagonal by way of the red edge it is necessary that the yellow upright
rhombus has an initial gradient U = α1 − (α0 − 1) > 0. The initial hive conditions also
imply that both α0 − 1 ≥ α − 1 and αs ≥ · · · ≥ α2 ≥ α1. It follows that α1 ≥ α0 and
αs ≥ · · · ≥ α2 ≥ α1 ≥ α so that after the path addition the upright rhombus has gradient
U − 1 = α1 − α0 ≥ 0 and the jth the right-leaning rhombus has gradient Rj = αj − α ≥ 0
for j = 1, 2, . . . , s. Thus all rhombus gradients remain ≥ 0. Not only that, the red edge
has an initial label that must be positive since U > 0, so it remains non-negative after
the path addition, and all hive conditions are therefore satisﬁed. However it still remains
to consider the left-leaning rhombus at the top of the ladder. To this end consider the
following diagram with the edge and gradient labels speciﬁed before the path addition.

In this situation, by hypothesis, the yellow upright rhombus has gradient U = β −β′ =

76

of the latter condition to equate two pairs of edge labels, one pair labelled α and the
other labelled β′′. The initial hive conditions also imply that α′ ≥ α and β′ ≥ β′′ from
which it follows that L = β − β′′ > 0 and R = α′′ − α > 0. After the path addition
the edge label β is reduced to β − 1 and the edge label α is increased to α + 1. All this
implies that the rhombus gradients U, L and R are reduced to U − 1, L − 1 and R − 1,
respectively, all of which remain ≥ 0. In addition the red edge label has been reduced
from β to β − 1 = U + β′ − 1 ≥ β′ which is necessarily non-negative by the initial hive
conditions. Thus yet again the hive conditions are preserved.

There is just one more concern that shows itself in the case of a type (iia) path
addition of the form illustrated on the right in (9.3), in which under the action of some
φk the path reaches the base of the hive in a diagonal j with k ≤ j < r and then proceeds
along this base to the right-hand corner. Our previous arguments are suﬃcient to see that,
after the addition of the type (iia) path, all hive rhombus gradient conditions are satisﬁed,
including the non-negativity of the upright rhombus gradient signiﬁed by U in (9.3), except
possibly the non-negativity of the gradient of the left-leaning rhombus of the penultimate
type indicated in (9.6) that appears in the bottom right hand corner of (9.3). All the
previous arguments about the preservation of hive conditions have been independent of
any positivity assumption about the gradient, L, of this left-leaning rhombus on the base
of the hive at its right-hand end. To meet the hive condition for this rhombus we require
that L = νr−1 −U ≥ 0 because at this stage, just before the application of χ νr
r , the bottom
two edges on the right-hand boundary have labels νr−1 and 0. Since the red edge labels
are weakly decreasing from left to right both before and after the path addition, all such
red edge labels will be non-negative if νr−1 − U ≥ 0. Thus the non-negativity of both L
and all red edge labels will be guaranteed after each type (iia) path addition if U ≤ νr−1.
However, since U increases steadily under these path additions from its initial value 0 to
its ﬁnal value ˜Ur−1,r, we need only ˜Ur−1,r ≤ νr−1 to ensure that the hive conditions are
satisﬁed at the conclusion of all type (ii) path additions.

We will actually need to ensure that a stronger condition is met. This is necessitated

by a consideration of the ﬁnal type (iii) path additions arising from the action of χ νr

The path P is generated by the action of φk with k < r and consists of a

10 Hive proofs of the bijective and involutive prop-

erties

The relationship between our path removal and path addition operations allows us to
establish the bijective nature of the maps we have encountered in Theorems 8.4 and 9.3
whose domains we can combine as follows:
Deﬁnition 10.1. For ﬁxed positive integer n, let H(n) be the union of all H(n)(λ, µ, ν) for
partitions λ, µ and ν with ℓ(λ) ≤ n and µ, ν ⊆ λ with |λ| = |µ|+|ν|. Let σ(n) : H(n) → H(n)
be such that for each H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) we have σ(n) : H 7→ K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ) with
K = σ(n)H, as deﬁned in Theorem 8.4. Similarly, let σ(n) : H(n) → H(n) be such that for
each K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ) we have σ(n) : K 7→ H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) with H = σ(n)K, as deﬁned
in Theorem 9.3.

With this deﬁnition we have the following:

Theorem 10.2. The maps σ(n) and σ(n) are mutually inverse bijections.
Proof: It follows from Theorems 8.4 and 9.3 that the maps σ(n) and σ(n) provide maps
from H(n)(λ, µ, ν) to H(n)(λ, ν, µ) and from H(n)(λ, ν, µ) to H(n)(λ, µ, ν), respectively.
Moreover, for any H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) if σ(n)H = K with K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ), then K records
the boundary edges of a sequence of path removals that reduce H to the empty hive H (0).

81

If K is then used to specify the sequence of path additions that create σ(n)K from H (0),
then this latter sequence is exactly the reverse of the original sequence with each path
removal replaced by its inverse in the form of a path addition. The sequence formed
in this way is precisely what is required to re-create H from H (0). That is to say, if
σ(n)H = K then σ(n)K = H. By exactly the same type of argument, if one starts with
K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ) and creates H = σ(n)K ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν), then the action of σ(n) on H
consists of reversing the order of the path additions and applying their inverses to H in
the form of corresponding path removals. By this means one necessarily arrives back at
K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ) as a record of the boundary edges of the sequence of path removals.
That is to say, this time, if H = σ(n)K then K = σ(n)H. It follows that σ(n) and σ(n) are
mutually inverse maps and that both are bijective.
(cid:3)

Our next task is to prove that the map σ(n) is an involution. To do this we proceed
by way of a sequence of Lemmas, in connection with which we need to introduce a new
type (iv) path removal operation.

i < n, the type (iv) path removal operator ψn acts on H by decreasing the red edge
labels by 1 along a zig-zag path from the edge labelled λn to that labelled νk where
k = min{j | Ujn > 0} as illustrated below. For convenience we have set βk,n−1 = β∗ and
Ukn = U ∗ with U ∗ > 0 by the deﬁnition of k,

the same for both H and ˆH.

if in the (n − 1)th diagonal two upright rhombus gradients at levels k and l separated by
0’s take values X ≥ 0 and Y ≥ 1 in ˆH (n−1), then they take the values X + 1 and Y − 1
in H (n−1), with further diﬀerences of ±1 in the foot and head rhombus gradients in four
more diagonals.

(n − 1)th diagonal in the form of a type (iii) path leaving an upright rhombus gradient of

X + 1 in H (n−1) and decreasing the top-most edge label by 1.

Furthermore, if the blue path reaches the left-hand boundary at level j ≤ n − 1
(counted from the bottom), then the path removals discussed so far create identical entries
Vin ≥ 0 in both K (1) and ˆK (1) for all i < j, together with Vjn = V + 1 in K (1) and
Vjn = V in ˆK (1), respectively, for some V ≥ 0, as illustrated in (10.5) on the right.
It can then be seen from this diagram that the removal of the brown path from K (1)
gives φn K (1) = ˆK (1) since the eﬀect of this removal is to reduce the lowest non-vanishing
upright rhombus gradient V + 1 to V . This proves the validity of (10.4) in the case r = 1.

Next we consider the removal of paths in ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1) through the action of
θn−1. There are two cases to consider: Case (i), in which all type (ii) paths removed from
ˆH (n−1) that enter the (n − 2)th diagonal at or below level k pass west and northwest to
the hive boundary without ever becoming coincident (in the sense of having an edge in
common) with the blue path; and Case (ii) in which at least one such path removed from
ˆH (n−1) does become coincident with the blue path. 1ex
Case (i) Consider ﬁrst all paths removed from ˆH (n−1) that enter the (n − 2)th diagonal
at or below level k to leave a hive ˆH ′. The eﬀect of their removal is to reduce the upright
rhombus gradient Uk,n−1 in ˆH (n−1) from X to 0 in ˆH ′. By hypothesis none of these paths
becomes coincident with the blue path. They pass to the left-hand boundary at level
i < j along paths determined by the upright rhombus gradients below the blue path,
with the foot rhombus gradients responsible for an inpenetrable barrier to climbing the
blue ladders. Exactly the same paths may be removed from H (n−1) since the upright
rhombus gradients below the blue path in H (n−1) and ˆH (n−1) diﬀer only through the
foot rhombi of the blue ladders having a gradient 1 greater than those in ˆH (n−1), as
signiﬁed by the symbols +1 in each such foot rhombus as shown on the left in (10.5).
They thereby present exactly the same inpenetrable barrier as before, leaving the paths
themselves unaltered. Removing these paths from H (n−1) to give a hive H ′ reduces the
upright rhombus gradient Uk,n−1 in H (n−1) from X + 1 to 1 in H ′. In this situation with
the upright rhombus gradient 1 in H ′ and 0 in ˆH ′, we say that the rhombus itself is
pre-critical. This is signiﬁed in (10.6) below by placing the symbols 0 above 1 in such an
upright rhombus.

In the case of path removals from H ′, this leaves a single extra type (ii) path P
entering the (n − 2)th diagonal at level k, as shown in magenta and red on the left in
(10.6). Before its removal, the diﬀerence of +1 in the gradients of the foot rhombi of H ′
as compared with those of ˆH ′, ensures that they remain positive. It follows that the extra
path P stays strictly below the blue path and ends on the left hand boundary at level i
with i < j.

The next path removal from ˆH (n−1) has also been illustrated on the left on the as-
sumption that we are in the Y > 0 situation. It takes the form of a type (ii) path ˆP and
proceeds up the (n − 1)th diagonal, following ﬁrst the magenta path and then the black
edge at level k across what is now the critical rhombus in the (n−1)th diagonal. After this
it necessarily follows the blue path that formed the only diﬀerence between ˆH (n−1) and
H (n−1). Thus the critical rhombus has produced a bifurcation of the magenta path into
the blue and red paths followed by ˆP and P , respectively. Thereafter the rhombus has
gradient 0 in both cases and is said to be post-critical. All the remaining pairs of paths
removed from ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1) coincide, each passing directly through the post-critical
rhombus.

On the other hand if we are in the Y = 0 situation, the blue path that formed the
only diﬀerence between ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1) would have proceeded directly up the (n − 1)th
diagonal to its top-most edge, with no diﬀerence between the upright rhombus gradients
of ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1) in this diagonal above level k, or indeed any other diagonal at any
level. It follows that all the remaining type (ii) path removals from ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1)
coincide. This leaves just type (iii) path removals from ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1), all following
the same path directly up the (n − 1)th diagonal, but with one more type (iii) path
removal required for ˆH (n−1) than for H (n−1). This extra path removal, like all the others
of type (iii), must also follow the blue path terminating at level j = n − 1, bringing the
edge labels along this path into coincidence for both ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1).

To conclude, for both Y = 0 and Y > 0 all the path removals from H (n−1) and ˆH (n−1)
coincide other than the magenta and red path removed from H (n−1) and the magenta,
black and blue path removed from ˆH (n−1). Since the blue part was already absent in
H (n−1) by virtue of its removal from H (n) it follows that ˆH (n−2) diﬀers from H (n−2) only
through diﬀerences of ±1 as appropriate in the labels of the red edges, together with
associated diﬀerences of ±1 in the gradients of the foot and head rhombi of the red
ladders in the diagonals from n − 2 down to i.

It also follows that if the numbers of paths reaching i and j in passing from H (n−1) to

H (n−2) are A and B, respectively, then the corresponding numbers for the passage from
ˆH (n−1) to ˆH (n−2) are A − 1 and B + 1, respectively, with A ≥ 1 and B ≥ 0. There are
no paths in either case reaching levels between i and j. The consequence for this for the
upright rhombus gradients of K (2) and ˆK (2) is illustrated on the right in (10.6), where the
entries A, B and V + 1 apply to K (2) and A − 1, B + 1 and V apply to ˆK (2). If we clarify
this by separating the diagrams of K (2) and ˆK (2) we ﬁnd that they are related by a single
path removal as shown below in (10.7).

It follows from the above argument that φn K (2) = ˆK (2) for all Case (i) examples, thereby
establishing for Case (i) the validity of (10.4) in the case r = 2.

Case (ii) By hypothesis, in this case there exists at least one type (ii) path removed
from ˆH (n−1) that enters the (n − 2)th diagonal at level k or below and becomes coincident
with the blue path that diﬀerentiates between ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1). This cannot occur, of
course, if this blue path is conﬁned, as in the Y = 0 situation, to the (n − 1)th diagonal.
Thus in Case (ii) we must be in the Y > 0 situation.

We then proceed as before by considering pairs of path removals generated by the
action of θn−1 on ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1). Let the sequence of pairs of type (ii) paths generated
in this way be denoted by ˆPa and Pa for ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1), respectively, with a =
1, 2, . . . , m. Here m is the sum of upright rhombus gradients in the (n − 1)th diagonal,
which is the same for both ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1). As we work up the diagram, the two paths
ˆPa and Pa, in ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1) respectively, are identical so long as the path ˆPa stays
below the route of the blue path, due to the same reason as in Case (i). However, by
virtue of our Case (ii) hypothesis, at least one of the paths ˆPa that enter the (n − 2)th
diagonal at or below level k, that is to say below the blue path, becomes coincident with
the blue path somewhere to its west. Let ˆPc be the ﬁrst such path. We shall now see
that this is a case of bifurcation between the paths ˆPc and Pc upon encountering a critical
rhombus.

Due to the form taken by type (ii) paths passing continuously from one diagonal to
another after climbing a ladder in each diagonal, the coincidence of ˆPc with the blue path

87

necessarily starts with the path ˆPc entering the blue ladder in, say, the pth diagonal,
traversing its foot rhombus at, say, level f , from its lower right edge, via its horizontal
In the picture (10.8)below, the part of ˆPc up to this foot
edge to its upper left edge.
rhombus is shown in dotted magenta, and the horizontal edge of this rhombus in black.
In order for this to happen, this rhombus must have gradient 0 in this situation. On the
other hand, the path Pc, initially coincident with ˆPc along the dotted magenta path, ﬁnds
that the gradient of the same foot rhombus equal to 1 instead of 0, a diﬀerence arising
from the blue path distinguishing H (n−1) from ˆH (n−1), and the path Pc therefore passes
west below this rhombus. The situation is marked in (10.8),as in Case (i), by placing the
symbols 0 above 1 in this rhombus, showing its gradients before the removals of ˆPc and
Pc respectively. When reached by the common dotted magenta part of the paths ˆPc and
Pc, it causes a bifurcation, so this rhombus should duly be called critical at this point.
Note that this rhombus will carry a common gradient 0 after this pair of removal, and we
shall then call it post-critical as in Case (i).

To follow the bifurcated paths beyond the critical rhombus, the path ˆPc necessarily
follows the route of the original blue path to its end at level j, since the upright rhombus
gradients guiding this route, namely those in the original blue ladders except for their
foot rhombi, have the same values as they did immediately before the removal of the
original blue path during the application of the operator θn to H (n), which was missing
in the application of θn to ˆH (n). This part of the original blue path is shown in dotted
blue in (10.8). The path Pc, on the other hand, after decreasing the gradient of the
critical rhombus from 1 to 0, proceeds along what is exempliﬁed as the red path in (108),
to end on the left-hand boundary at some level i with i < j, for the same reason as in
Case (i) regarding an inpenetrable barrier below the original blue path.

Let ˆH and H be the hives obtained from ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1), respectively, at this stage.
The only edges of ˆH and H that have diﬀerent labels constitute what we call a line of
diﬀerence. Working from right to left, this consists of the undotted blue edges, the single
black edge and the red edges, each of whose edge labels diﬀers by ±1 as between ˆH and
H. For future reference the black edge label in ˆH is one greater than that in H.
In
general there remain further path removals from both ˆH and H under the action of θn−1.

88

With an additional assumption on the subsequent pairs of paths generated by the
action of θn−1 that none of them encounter any critical rhombi except in the starting
(n − 1)th diagonal, such a pair of path removals from both ˆH and H emanating from
levels at or below k must proceed together weakly above the dotted magenta path and
strictly below the blue path until they enter the pth diagonal at a level below (that is
geometrically below) level f . Thereafter they follow the dotted magenta path up the
pth diagonal, passing inexorably through the post-critical rhombus since its gradient is 0
in both ˆH and H. They then proceed west or northwest weakly above the dotted blue
path, eventually terminating on the left-hand boundary at level j or above. Since, by
crossing the line of diﬀerence at the post-critical rhombus they do not disturb it, these
path removals from ˆH and H necessarily coincide, and they still leave the black edge
having labels that diﬀer by 1 for ˆH and H.

However, because of the distinction between the upright rhombus gradients X + 1 and
Y −1 in the (n−1)th diagonal of H (n−1) at levels k and l, and the corresponding gradients
X and Y in ˆH (n−1), the next pair of paths Pd and ˆPd removed from H (n−1) and ˆH (n−1),
respectively, are no longer coincident. As illustrated below in (10.9), they are initially
coincident along a magenta section in the (n − 1)th diagonal, until they both meet the
pre-critical rhombus at level k. Here they diverge, with Pd following a red path strictly
below the original blue path as far as the post-critical rhombus in the pth diagonal at
level q, with ˆPd crossing the critical rhombus in its (n−1)th diagonal by means of its black
horizontal edge, and then following the dotted blue path, coincident with a portion of
the original blue path, as far once again as the post-critical rhombus in the pth diagonal
at level q. At that point the path Pd crosses this post-critical rhombus along what had
been in (10.8) its black horizontal edge and merges with the path ˆPd, with both then
proceeding along a magenta path to the left-hand boundary at some level j′ ≥ j.

Now let ˆH ′ and H ′ be the hives obtained from ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1), respectively, at

along the horizontal edge of the critical rhombus in the (n − 1)th diagonal changes the

89

edge label in ˆH ′ so that it is one lower than that in H. It is therefore coloured black in
(10.9) to signify that it is now to be incorporated in the line of diﬀerence between ˆH ′ and
H ′. In contrast to this, the fact that Pd, but not ˆPd, passes along the horizontal edge of
the critical rhombus in the pth diagonal of H lowers its edge label by 1, bringing it into
coincidence with that of ˆH ′ and thereby removing this edge from the line of diﬀerence
between ˆH ′ and H ′. This new line of diﬀerence thus consists of the single blue and black
edges in (10.9), together with the red edges.

Continuing to remove the remaining Y − 1 paths neceassary to reduce to 0 the upright
rhombus gradient at level l in diagonal n − 1, has no eﬀect on this line of diﬀerence
since the paths are coincident in ˆH ′ and H ′, as indeed are all subsequent path removals
generated by the action of θn−1, including those of type (iii). All such paths pass up the
(n−1)th diagonal to level l, crossing the line of diﬀerence without disturbing it in any way
at level k, before proceeding either west and northwest or just northwest to the left-hand
hive boundary at or above level j′.

As in Case (i) this leave H (n−2) and ˆH (n−2) diﬀering by a single path removal, namely
that speciﬁed this time by the red part of the line of diﬀerence that lies within these two
(n − 2)-hives, with K (2) and ˆK (2) diﬀering exactly as before by the single brown path
removal illustrated in (10.7). This conﬁrms the validity of ˆK (2) = ψn K (2) in all Case (ii)
examples for which there is just one interior critical rhombus in addition to the critical
rhombus on the right-hand boundary.

However, there exist other Case (ii) examples involving more than one internal critical
rhombus as will be exempliﬁed next. The elucidation of paths proceeds as before. There
may exist an initial sequence of pairs of paths common to ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1) that pass
to the left-hand boundary without meeting the original blue path responsible for the
distinction between these two hives. Then by hypothesis there exists a pair of paths Pc
and ˆPc that follow a dotted magenta path along a common route in ˆH (n−1) and H (n−1)
until this reaches the ﬁrst critical rhombus in the pth diagonal. Here it bifurcates, with
ˆPc following the dotted blue path in ˆH (n−1) to the left-hand boundary at level j and Pc
following the red path in H (n−1) to the same boundary at level i. Thereby leaving the
following post-critical situation in which the pair of upright rhombus gradients have both
been shown to be 0.

It is clear that this argument may be extended to the case of an arbitrary number of
internal critical rhombi, through the insertion of additional pairs of paths that initially
coincide, then bifurcate at some critical point before meeting at the next interior point to
the west or northwest, crossing the line of diﬀerence and thereafter merging and passing
to the left hand boundary, precisely as in (10.11). This still leaves ˆK (2) = φn K (2) in all
cases, conﬁrming the validity of (10.4) in the case r = 2.

To extend this argument to show that ˆK (r) = φn K (r) for r > 2 it should be noted
that, just as H (n−1) diﬀers from ˆH (n−1) only by the removal of a single blue path entering

Iterating this process allows one to see that H (s) diﬀers from ˆH (s) only by the removal
of a single magenta path entering the s-hive region at level k and leaving at some level
h(s), again as shown above in (10.14). This continues until the case s = k, exempliﬁed
by the removal of the green path that distinguishes H (k) from ˆH (k).

Now we claim that H (k−1) = ˆH (k−1). Upon application of θk, the hive H (k) aﬀords
one extra type (i) path removal compared with ˆH (k), whereas ˆH (k) aﬀords one extra type
(ii) path removal compared with H (k), whose route coincides with the green path, with
its rightmost β edge, that is to say the edge that would initially have been labelled βkk,
replaced by the bottom γ edge labelled λk. Thus the pairs of path removals via θk,
applied to H (k) and ˆH (k), eliminate the diﬀerence shown by the green path, yielding
H (k−1) = ˆH (k−1). Hereafter it is obvious that the pairs of path removals coincide for both
hives all the way to the empty hive H (0) = ˆH (0).

All this is reﬂected in the partner hive arena. The algorithm leading from K (0) and

previously removed from H (n). The corresponding gradients of rhombi in the (n − 1)th

11 Observations on the centrality of upright rhombus

gradients

Here we brieﬂy mention some ideas that come to mind in reviewing the structure of the
hive path removal procedure. In deﬁning the operator θr = ωµr
r as in (7.15),
its action on H (r) is such that the operators χr and ωr not only mutually commute, but
also commute with each operator φr. In fact the actions of χr or ωr do not change any
upright rhombus gradients, causing no change in the way each operator φr, driven solely
by the upright rhombus gradients, ﬁnds its path, modiﬁes such gradients and arrives at
its terminating row number.

12 Discussion of the coincidence of commutativity bi-

jections

We review here the coincidence of the fundamental symmetry maps ρ1, ρ2, ρ′
2 in [PV10],
the Henriques–Kamnitzer crystal commutor [HK06a] and hive commutor [HK06b], as well

101

as the commutativity bijection of Danilov and Koshevoy [DK05, DK08] on arrays. Our
own commutativity bijections on tableaux and hives both correspond to the fundamen-
tal symmetry map referred to as ρ3 in [PV10]. As we have pointed out in Section 2,
Littlewood–Richardson coeﬃcients may be evaluated by means of Gelfand–Tsetlin pat-
terns as in [GZ86] and [BZ89]. The ﬁrst method amounts to exploiting a bijection, τ ,
between LR tableaux of shape λ/µ and weight ν, and GT patterns of type ν and weight
λ − µ satisfying a µ-dominance condition, as in (2.32), which is equivalent to the non-
negativity of the right leaning rhombus gradients Rij, as on the left hand side of (2.17),
and the second method amounts to exploiting another bijection, γ, between LR tableaux
of shape λ/µ and weight ν, and GT patterns of type µ and weight the reverse of λ−ν satis-
fying a condition, as in (2.33), which is equivalent to the non-negativity of the left leaning
rhombus gradients Lij, as on the right hand side of (2.17). See also [KB96, HK06b, PV10].
In [PV10], Section 4.5, the deﬁnitions of bijections, τ and γ are expressed in terms of semi-
standard Young tableaux rather than Gelfand–Tsetlin patterns. However, these diﬀerent
formulations can be readily translated into the language of hives since the relevant GT
patterns are precisely those exempliﬁed by G(α)
in (2.21) augmented by appro-
priate missing right and left hand boundary edge labels, as will be illustrated below.

µ and G(β)
ν

12.1 Littlewood-Richardson rule, gln-crystal bases and crystal

commutor

Following [KTW04b] and using gln-crystal bases, Henriques and Kamnitzer studied, in
[HK06a], the hive model for the general linear Lie algebra, gln, tensor products, and
established relations between hives, tableaux, Gelfand–Tsetlin patterns, and crystal bases.
Crystal bases of integrable representations of the quantum group Uq(gln) [Kas91] enjoy
nice properties. In the remainder of this subsection, Greek letters λ, µ or ν will be used
to represent partitions of length ≤ n. For each such λ, let Bλ denote the crystal basis
of the irreducible representation Vλ of Uq(gln). The crystal tensor product Bµ ⊗ Bν gives
a crystal basis of the tensor product of the representations Vµ ⊗ Vν of Uq(gln), and its
decomposition into connected components comes from a direct sum decomposition of
Vµ ⊗ Vν into irreducible Uq(gln)-submodules. Each connected component of Bµ ⊗ Bν is
isomorphic to some Bλ as a crystal, and each Bλ has a unique highest weight element of
weight λ and a unique lowest weight element of weight rev(λ) = w0λ, where w0 is the
longest element of the Weyl group. Thus we may evaluate cλ
µν by counting the connected
components in Bµ ⊗Bν isomorphic to Bλ, or equivalently, the highest (resp. lowest) weight
elements of weight λ (resp. rev(λ)) in Bµ ⊗ Bν.

There is a “reversing” map ξ [HK06a] acting on any disjoint union of the crystals of
the form Bλ, including Bµ ⊗ Bν, preserving each connected component but exchanging
its highest and lowest weight elements. In [HK06a] it was shown that it is an involution
using a certain uniqueness theorem on crystals.

In the case of gln, we can take Bλ to be the set of all semistandard tableaux of shape
λ, in the alphabet {1, . . . , n}, suitably equipped with crystal operators [Nak93]. Then the
highest weight element is the Yamanouchi tableau Yλ, exempliﬁed on the left below, and

102

the lowest weight element ξ(Yλ), exempliﬁed on the right below. The map ξ coincides
with the Sch¨utzenberger involution acting on semistandard tableaux.

For the sake of comparison, we note that in [HK06b] the order of tensor factors is
reverse to, say, the one used in [Kas91, Nak93] which we adopt here; compare [HK06b, p.

of hives in [HK06b] employ an orientation diﬀerent from that adopted here.

The tensor product of crystals is not symmetric in the sense that the map from A ⊗ B
to B ⊗A sending each a⊗b to b⊗a does not commute with the crystal operators. Instead,
Henriques and Kamnitzer [HK06a] show that the map σA,B : a ⊗ b 7→ ξ(ξ(b) ⊗ ξ(a)) does
give a crystal isomorphism from A ⊗ B to B ⊗ A, and that it has the involutive nature
expressed by σB,AσA,B = 1. Through this isomorphism applied to Bµ ⊗ Bν, they deﬁne
an involution ComHK : LR(λ/µ, ν) → LR(λ/ν, µ), sending each T to T ∗ say, in such a
way that σBµ,Bν (BT ) = BT ∗ (note that BT ⊂ Bµ ⊗ Bν while BT ∗ ⊂ Bν ⊗ Bµ). Since

12.2 LR commutativity bijections and their coincidence

In [PV10], it is conjectured that ρ1, ρ2, and ρ′
2, the inverse of ρ2, and the fundamental sym-
metry map ρ3, described in [PV10], deﬁned originally in [Aze99, Aze00] and fully detailed
here as ρ(n), coincide. From the gln-crystal commutor arguments above we know that
ρ2 = ρ′
2 = ComHK. Using a special case of octahedron recurrence in [KTW04b],
Henriques and Kamnitzer construct a bijective commutor, Comh
HK, from hives of bound-
ary (λ, µ, ν) to those of boundary (λ, ν, µ) [HK06a]. The bijection starts with a hive H

2 = ρ−1

and consists of a series of intermediate stages whose intermediate objects are not hives. It
becomes a hive H ∗ again in the last step. The involution property is not proved directly.
It is shown that in the equivalent language of GT patterns or semistandard tableaux,
intermediate stages correspond to a sequence of Bender–Knuth moves [BK72] applied to
T (α)

that Comh

f (n)), from which it follows that the Henriques–Kamnitzer hive commutor Comh
HK is also
an involution. Therefore Comh

2 .

In [DK05] Danilov and Koshevoy introduce a theory of arrays suited to discrete concave
functions (hives) on the lattice of integers as well as to Young tableaux. The set of
arrays is then equipped with the crystal structure and the tensor product of crystal
arrays is considered. Using the Sch¨utzenberger involution, a commutor for the tensor
product of arrays is introduced. Then it is shown that it coincides with Henriques–
Kamnitzer commutor as well as with the bijections ρ1, ρ2 and ρ′
2 deﬁned in terms of
Young tableaux. This proves the Pak–Vallejo aforementioned conjecture leaving ρ3 out.
The main philosophy in [Aze08] is to claim that ρ3 can be seen as providing a short cut of
tableau-switching when this procedure is applied to LR tableaux, and thereby it coincides
with ρ1.

13 Concluding remarks

We have given direct combinatorial proofs, using two distinct models, of the bijective and
involutive nature of a procedure ﬁrst introduced by Azenhas [Aze99, Aze00] as a means

105

of establishing combinatorially the symmetry of Littlewood–Richardson coeﬃcients. The
ﬁrst model was based on the use of Littlewood–Richardson tableaux and followed Azen-
has’s procedure in providing a commutativity operator, denoted here by ρ(n), mapping
a given LR tableau T ∈ LR(λ/µ, ν) to a new LR tableau S ∈ LR(λ/ν, µ), thereby
preserving the outer shape while interchanging the inner shape and the weight. The
transformation from T to S consisted of a sequence of deletions from T , starting from
right to left in each row taken in turn from bottom to top, with the new tableau S con-
structed by arranging the terminating row numbers obtained from the deletions in the
form of a tableau, starting from its bottom row and proceeding upwards, as exempliﬁed
in Example 4.4. The second model was based on the use of Littlewood–Richardson hives,
on which we deﬁned a corresponding commutativity operator which we denoted by σ(n).
It transforms a given LR hive H ∈ H(n)(λ, µ, ν) to a new LR hive K ∈ H(n)(λ, ν, µ) by
the application of what we called path removals from H, working again from right to
left, with each path starting from the base of the hive, and recording within K the level
reached by each path, as exemplifed this time in Example 8.3.

Although it was not stated explicitly in the text, it should be understood that the two
procedures, one in the realm of tableaux and the other in that of hives, are intimately
related at every stage by the bijective correspondence f (n) between LR tableaux and
LR hives, as described in Section 2.3. Nevertheless, we have been at pains to keep the
two procedures self-contained, without interrupting them in Sections 3-6 and Sections 7-
10 with repetitious accounts of the connection between them.
In fact each of the two
formulations has its own particular merits and the development of the argument in the
tableaux setting has beneﬁtted from insights obtained in the hive setting and vice versa.
